The Meaning of 'Twenty Buckets' in Well Purification: Grammatical and Legal Clarification
Critical refinement of a grammatical and interpretative issue in well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the grammatical issue with the phrase 'وعشرون' in the well purification ruling and apply the author's resolution that the waw means 'or', distinguishing the different impurity scenarios.

Core Idea
The waw in 'وعشرون' means 'or', not 'and' — each phrase specifies a distinct amount for a different impurity.

In this passage, the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a grammatical problem in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the ruling on drawing water from a well when an impurity occurs. The phrase 'عشرون' (twenty buckets) appears as a conjunct of 'البئر' (the well), leading to a potential contradiction. The commentator proposes a simpler reading: the waw means 'or', thereby presenting alternative scenarios. This clarification preserves the legal meaning and removes ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical problem
'عشرون' is a conjunct of 'البئر', so the literal reading would require drawing both the well's entire water and twenty buckets simultaneously — an impossibility.
The matn says 'وينزح البئر وعشرون دلواً وأربعون دلواً وكلها'.
2
Understand al-Zayla'i's interpretation
Al-Zayla'i argued that 'البئر' is ambiguous, referring to the category of impurities requiring all water, so 'عشرون' is a subset. But he saw a problem with potential repetition.
He said if the first meant all, then listing forty and all again would be repetition.
3
Learn the author's resolution
The waw in 'وعشرون' should be interpreted as 'or' (أو), making the entire list a series of mutually exclusive cases.
This is a recognized usage of waw in Arabic.
4
Apply the resolution to the full set
The complete reading: non-animal impurity → draw all water; mouse → twenty buckets; chicken → forty; sheep → all water (for a different reason).
Thus 'the well' in the first phrase means water of the well, and the waw-as-'or' separates the cases.
5
Note the dismissal of objections
The author refutes al-Zayla'i's claim that the first cannot apply to a type; he shows it applies to a fourth type (non-animal impurity), so no repetition.
The critique is methodological: al-Zayla'i's reasoning is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعشرون) دلواً وسطا بموت نحو (فأرة قال في التبيين : أي ينزح عشرون إذا ماتت فيها فأرة ونحوه وقوله عشرون معطوف على البئر وفيه إشكال وهو أنه تنزح البئر وعشرون دلواً وأربعون وكله فيفسد المعنى لأنه يقتضي نزح البئر وعشرين دلواً بمراد، وإنما المراد أن تنزح البئر إذا وقع فيها نجس. ثم ذلك النجس ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : منه ما يوجب نزح عشرين، ومنه ما يوجب نزح أربعين، ومنه ما يوجب نزح الجميع. وليس نزح البئر مغايراً لهذه الثلاث حتى يعطف عليها وإنما هو تفسير وتقسيم لذلك النزح المبهم، وليس هذا من باب عطف البعض على الكل لا يقال إنه أراد بالأول ما يوجب الجميع وبالمعطوف ما يوجب نزح البعض لأنه ذكر بعد ذلك ما يوجب نزح الجميع أيضاً، فلو كان مراده الجميع لما ذكر ثانياً لكونه تكراراً محضاً، ولأن الأول لا يجوز أن يعمل على نوع من هذه الأنواع الثلاثة لعدم الأولوية فبقي على إطلاقه. إلى هنا كلام الزيلعي رحمه الله وأقول : لا حاجة إلى هذه الإطالة مع إمكان حمل كلامه على وجه صحيح، فإن قوله «عشرون» معطوف على البئر) بمعنى ماء البئر كما تقدم والواو فيه كبقية المعطوفات بمعنى «أو» والتقدير ينزح ماء البئر كله بوقوع نجس غير حيوان، أو ينزح عشرون دلواً من ماء البئر بموت نحو فأرة، أو أربعون منه بنحو دجاجة، أو كله بتحو شاة إلى آخره، وبهذا علم أن قوله وتنزح البئر بوقوع نجس ليس مبهماً بل المراد منه نجس غير حيوان، واندفع به ما ذكره من لزوم التكرار لو أريد بالأول نزح الجميع فإنه أريد بالأول نزح الجميع لوقوع غير حيوان، وأريد بالثاني نزح الجميع لوقوع حيوان مخصوص فلا تكرار. وقوله «ولأن الأوّل لا يجوز أن يحمل إلى آخره سلمناه لكن يمنع قوله فبقين على إطلاقه» لأنه لا يلزم من جواز حمله على الأنواع الثلاث بقاؤه مطلقاً لجواز حمله على نوع رابع غير الثلاثة كما حملناه انتفاء فجوازه باليقين اتفاقي كما صرح به في المصفى لقصة العرنيين .اهـ. قوله : (وقول محمد مشكل الخ) قال في النهر : مدفوع إذ الكلام في طاهر لا إيذاء فيه بل كان دواء على أن المنع في لبن الأتان ممنوع ففي البزازية لا بأس بالتداوي به قال الصدر وفيه نظر قوله : (لا) إشكال فيه) أي في قول أبي يوسف: قوله : (كالمسلم المغسول أو (الشهيد قال في الشرنبلالية فيه نظر لما أن الدم الذي به غير على النجس الذي ليس حيواناً وهو ليس واحداً من الأنواع
His saying: (And twenty) medium buckets upon the death of something like a mouse. He said in al-Tabyin: meaning twenty are drawn when a mouse or similar dies in it. And his saying twenty is a conjunct of the well, and there is a difficulty in it: that it implies that the well and twenty buckets and forty and all are drawn, which corrupts the meaning because it requires drawing the well and twenty buckets at once. Rather, the intended meaning is that the well is drawn when impurity falls into it. Then that impurity is divided into three sections: from it what necessitates drawing twenty, from it what necessitates drawing forty, and from it what necessitates drawing all. And the drawing of the well is not different from these three such that it is conjoined to them; rather, it is an explanation and division of that ambiguous drawing. And this is not from the chapter of conjoining a part to the whole. It cannot be said that he meant by the first what necessitates all, and by the conjunct what necessitates drawing some, because he mentioned after that what necessitates drawing all as well. So if he meant all, he would not have mentioned a second time as it would be pure repetition. And because the first cannot be applied to a type of these three types due to lack of priority, so it remains in its absolute sense. Thus far is the speech of al-Zayla'i, may Allah have mercy on him. And I say: There is no need for this length, given the possibility of interpreting his words in a correct manner. For his saying 'twenty' is a conjunct of 'the well' meaning the water of the well as previously, and the waw is, like the other conjuncts, in the meaning of 'or'. The estimated meaning is: 'The water of the well is entirely drawn upon the occurrence of impurity that is not an animal, or twenty buckets of the water of the well are drawn upon the death of something like a mouse, or forty from it upon something like a chicken, or all of it upon something like a sheep' etc. By this it is known that his saying 'and the well is drawn upon the occurrence of impurity' is not ambiguous; rather, what is meant by it is impurity that is not an animal. And there is repelled what he mentioned of the necessity of repetition if by the first was meant drawing all, for by the first was meant drawing all upon the occurrence of a non-animal, and by the second was meant drawing all upon the occurrence of a specific animal, so there is no repetition. And his saying 'and because the first cannot be applied... etc.' we grant it, but his saying 'so it remains in its absolute sense' is prevented because the permissibility of applying it to the three types does not necessitate its remaining absolute, due to the permissibility of applying it to a fourth type other than the three, as we have applied it — the negation — so its permissibility by certainty is agreed upon, as explained in al-Musaffa concerning the story of the 'Urayniyys. End. His saying: (And Muhammad's statement is problematic etc.) He said in al-Nahr: it is refuted because the discussion concerns something pure that has no harm, rather it was a medicine; and the prohibition of the milk of a female donkey is denied, for in al-Bazzaziyya: there is no harm in treating with it. Al-Sadr said: there is consideration in it. His saying: (No) there is no problem in it, i.e., in Abu Yusuf's statement. His saying: (Like the washed Muslim or the martyr) He said in al-Shurumbulalaliyya: there is consideration in it because the blood that is on it is not upon the impurity that is not an animal, and it is not one of the types.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) from the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, discussing the interpretation of a phrase in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the amount of water to be drawn from a well when an impurity occurs. The commentator al-Zayla'i raised a grammatical problem: if 'twenty buckets' is a conjunct of 'the well', the syntax seems to imply that both the well itself (i.e., all its water) and twenty buckets are drawn, which is contradictory. Al-Zayla'i's own resolution is that 'the well' here is ambiguous, referring to the category of impurity that necessitates drawing all. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq proposes a simpler solution: the waw (و) in 'and twenty' means 'or' (أو), and the phrase is a disjunction of different scenarios. He provides a complete interpretive reading: 'The water of the well is entirely drawn upon the occurrence of impurity that is not an animal, or twenty buckets are drawn upon the death of a mouse, or forty upon a chicken, or all upon a sheep.' This removes the ambiguity and any repetition. The passage also includes side comments about the permissibility of using donkey milk for medicine, based on the principle that prohibition is lifted for treatment, and about the distinction between a washed Muslim martyr and others.

Key Terms
عشرون دلواً ('ishrūn dalwan): Twenty buckets of well water to be drawn when a mouse or similar dies in the well.
بئر (bi'r): The well itself; in the phrase 'water of the well' is implied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Addition (jam')
B) Disjunction (aw)
C) Explanation (tafsir)
D) Emphasis (tawkid)

Key Takeaway
The waw in the well purification ruling is best understood as 'or', presenting alternative amounts for different impurities.

Rulings on Impurities and Animals Falling into a Well
Detailed classification of impurities and animals affecting well water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the effects of different animals and impurities falling into a well and state the Hanafi ruling on drawing water in each case.

Core Idea
The ruling on well water depends on the type of impurity or animal, with the principle that original purity requires clear evidence of spoilage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq systematically categorizes what falls into a well—impurity or animal—and gives detailed rulings for each. It also refutes the erroneous view that a dry mouse is pure because dryness acts like tanning. You will learn the Hanafi positions on humans, inherently impure animals, edible and non-edible animals, and the amounts of water to draw.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the categories of what falls into a well
The passage divides the fallen object into impurity (already known ruling) or animal. Animals are further divided into humans and non-humans, and non-humans into inherently impure, edible, non-edible, etc. This classification determines the ruling.
The ruling for pure impurity (non-animal) is that all water must be drawn.
2
2. State the ruling for each category
For each subclass, determine whether the water becomes impure and how much must be drawn. Key examples: a pure human alive does not spoil water; a disbeliever does; a dead Muslim after ghusl does not; a dog on the stronger view spoils only if its mouth touches water; an edible animal without known impurity does not spoil; a non-edible animal (e.g., predator) has ikhtilaf, and the correct view is no spoiling.
The amount for a mouse or sparrow is twenty buckets wajib, thirty mustahabb.
3
3. Analyze the proof for the dry mouse ruling
The passage refutes the claim that a dry mouse is pure because dryness tans. It uses a counterexample from al-Dhakhira: a dry mouse in oil makes the oil impure, proving dryness does not purify. This shows the Hanafi principle that impure objects remain impure unless physically purified.
This refutation relies on the broader rule that only tanning of hides purifies, not mere dryness.
4
4. Identify the ikhtilaf (disputed points)
There is ikhtilaf on whether the dog is inherently impure (the correct Hanafi view is it is not, and only if its mouth touches the water). Also, on non-edible animals and donkeys/mules, there is ikhtilaf among mashayikh, with the preferred (asahh) view being no spoiling or doubt.
The mufta bihi is that the dog is not inherently impure and non-edible animals do not spoil water.
5
5. Determine the mufta bihi and practical application
Summarize the operative rulings: twenty buckets wajib for mouse/sparrow, thirty mustahabb; dog not inherently impure; non-edible animals do not spoil; human rulings as stated. Apply to a modern scenario: a cow falls into a well—if no visible impurity, water remains pure.
The principle is original purity unless known impurity is confirmed.

الهداية .
And know that there is no difference between the mouse dying in the well or outside it and being thrown into it; likewise all other animals, except the dead upon which prayer is permitted, like the washed Muslim or the martyr. Yes, in Khizanat al-Fatawa: the dry mouse does not render the water impure because dryness is tanning. But its weakness is not hidden, for we have previously stated that what does not tolerate tanning is not purified, and dryness is not tanning. This is supported by what is in al-Dhakhira: the dead mouse, if it is dry and in a jar, and oil is put into the jar, and it appears on the surface of the jar, the oil is impure. End. Then know that what falls into the well is either impurity or an animal. The ruling of impurity has already preceded in his saying 'and the well is drawn upon the occurrence of impurity' as we have explained. The animal is either human or other. The non-human is either inherently impure or not. The not inherently impure is either edible meat or not. And all are either taken out alive or dead, and the dead is either swollen or not. The human: if taken out alive and there is no real or legal impurity on his body and he has performed istinja', he does not spoil the water. If he is a Muslim in a state of janaba or minor impurity and immerses with the intention of ghusl or to seek the bucket, his ruling has preceded. If he is a disbeliever, it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that its water is drawn because his body is not free from real or legal impurity. If taken out dead, and he is a Muslim who fell after ghusl, he does not spoil the water; if before ghusl, he spoils it. The disbeliever spoils before and after ghusl. The non-human, if inherently impure like the pig and the dog, according to the view that it is inherently impure, renders the well impure, whether dead or alive, whether its mouth touched the water or not. According to the view that the dog is not inherently impure, it does not render it impure if its mouth did not reach the water, and this is the more correct view. And it is said that its anus is turned outward, for this reason it spoils the water contrary to other animals. As for other animals, if impurity is known on its body, it renders the water impure even if its mouth does not reach the water. And we qualified with 'known' because they have said concerning a cow and the like: it comes out and nothing must be drawn, even though apparent is that its urine covers its thighs, but it is possible that it is pure because it fell after entering a lot of water. This is while the principle is purity. And if it is not known and its mouth did not reach the water, then if it is edible meat, it does not cause impurity at all. If it is not edible meat, like predators and birds, there is a difference of opinion among the mashayikh, and the most correct is that it does not cause impurity. Similarly for the donkey and the mule, the correct view is that the water does not become doubtful. And it is said that the entire water of the well is drawn. If its saliva reaches, the ruling of the water is the same as it: pure for others, except that it completes? Upon the case where it is washed off before falling into the well. His saying: (because it fell) i.e., the impurity; and the pronoun 'its falling' refers to the cow; and 'water' in the accusative is the object of 'falling'. His saying: (then it is necessary to draw all) I say: in the expression of al-Khaniyya there is not the word 'wajib'; rather it said 'the entire water is drawn'. Yes, its apparent meaning is obligation. And like the expression of al-Khaniyya is the expression of al-Hawi al-Qudsi and Munyat al-Musalli, and its commentator Ibn Amir Hajj attributed it to al-Bada'i. Likewise in al-Durar, its commentator Shaykh Isma'il attributed it to al-Mubtagha. His saying: (Twenty buckets are drawn from it, and the sparrow and similar equal the mouse in the body, so it takes its ruling. And twenty is obligatory, thirty is recommended. Thus in al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings concerning what falls into a well, categorizing impurities and animals. The author first addresses the ruling of a dry mouse: despite a view that dryness purifies, the author refutes it by stating that dryness is not tanning and cites al-Dhakhira as evidence. He then classifies the fallen object: either impurity (whose ruling is that the entire well is drawn, as previously stated for non-animal impurity) or an animal. Animals are divided into human and non-human. For a human: if alive and pure, the water remains pure; if a Muslim in janaba or without wudu, he does not spoil the water if he immersed with intention; if a disbeliever, the well is drawn due to certainty of impurity on his body. If dead: a Muslim who fell after being washed does not spoil; if before, he spoils; a disbeliever spoils in both cases. For non-human animals: those inherently impure (pig, dog on the stronger view) spoil the well regardless; on the weaker view, only if the mouth touches the water. For other animals: if known impurity on the body, it spoils; if uncertain, edible meat animals do not spoil; non-edible (predators, birds) are subject to ikhtilaf, with the preferred view being no spoiling. Donkeys and mules: the correct view is that the water does not become doubtful. The passage also clarifies that the word 'wajib' (obligatory) is not explicit in the texts; the ruling is that the water must be drawn. Finally, it states that a sparrow is similar to a mouse and requires twenty buckets, with thirty being recommended.

Key Terms
عشرون ('ishrūn): Twenty buckets: the obligatory amount when a mouse or similar dies in the well.
فأرة يابسة (fa'ra yābisa): A dry mouse; initially claimed to be pure because dryness tans, but this is rejected.
لعاب (lu'āb): Saliva of an animal; if it reaches the water, the ruling of the water is the same as the saliva (i.e., pure if the animal is pure).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Twenty buckets must be drawn.
D) The well becomes doubtful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on well water is based on a detailed classification of what falls in, with the principle that original purity holds unless a specific cause of impurity is confirmed.

Ruling on a Dry Mouse and Why Dryness Is Not Tanning
Refutation of dryness as tanning for impure animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water that contacts a dry dead animal and explain why dryness does not purify.

Core Idea
Dryness does not purify a dead animal; only proper tanning (dibagh) can remove impurity from hides.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the status of a dry mouse that falls into water. He strongly refutes an opinion found in Khizanat al-Fatawa that claims dryness itself can act as a tanning agent. Understanding this ruling is essential for knowing when water becomes impure and cannot be used for purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling for Any Dead Animal in Water
Begin by noting that the default ruling for a dead animal in water is that it renders the water impure, whether the animal died inside or outside the water. The only exception is a human corpse that is permissible to pray upon (e.g., a washed Muslim or a martyr).
This applies to all animals, not just mice.
2
Examine the Opposing View from Khizanat al-Fatawa
Read the opinion that a dry mouse does not make water impure because dryness is considered a form of tanning (dibagh). This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
Dryness here means the mouse is completely dry, not fresh.
3
Understand the Author's Refutation
The author rejects this view, stating that dryness is not tanning. He reminds us of a previous principle: any impurity that cannot undergo tanning (i.e., the whole animal, not just the skin) cannot be purified by dryness.
Tanning only works on skin, not the entire animal.
4
Apply the Evidence from al-Dhakhira
The author provides a concrete example: if a dry dead mouse is in a jar and oil is poured over it, the oil becomes impure. This proves that dryness does not remove impurity.
The oil appearing on top shows contact with the mouse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لا فرق بين أن تموت الفأرة في البئر أو خارجها وتلقى فيها وكذا سائر الحيوانات إلا الميت الذي تجوز الصلاة عليه كالمسلم المغسول أو الشهيد نعم في خزانة الفتاوى والفأرة اليابسة لا تنجس الماء لأن اليبس دباغة ا هـ . ولا يخفى ضعفه لأنا قدمنا أن ما لا يحتمل الدباغة لا يطهر وأن اليبس ليس بدباغة، ويدل عليه ما في الذخيرة أن الفأرة الميتة إذا كانت يابسة وهي في الخابية وجعل في الخابية الزيت فظهرت على رأس الخابية فالزيت نجس ا هـ .
And know that there is no difference between a mouse dying in the well or outside and being thrown into it, and likewise for all animals except a dead person upon whom prayer is permitted, such as a washed Muslim or a martyr. Yes, in Khizanat al-Fatawa: a dry mouse does not make water impure because dryness is tanning. And its weakness is not hidden because we have stated that what cannot be tanned is not purified and that dryness is not tanning. And evidence for it is what is in al-Dhakhira: if a dead mouse is dry and it is in a jar and oil is poured into the jar and it appears on top of the jar, the oil is impure.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of a dry mouse falling into water. The author refutes the opinion from Khizanat al-Fatawa that dryness acts as tanning and purifies the mouse. He argues that impurities which cannot be tanned do not become pure by dryness, and dryness is not a tanning process. He provides evidence from al-Dhakhira: a dry mouse in a jar of oil renders the oil impure, proving that dryness does not remove impurity. The Hanafi position is that the water becomes impure, and the opinion of Khizanat al-Fatawa is weak.

Key Terms
دباغة (dibāghah): Tanning process that purifies the skin of a dead animal.
يبس (yubs): Dryness; the state of being dry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because al-Dhakhira contradicts the Khizanat.

Key Takeaway
Dryness does not purify a dead animal; water that contacts a dry dead animal becomes impure (najis) and cannot be used for purification.

Rulings for a Human Falling into a Well
Rulings for human falling into well based on state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a human falling into a well based on their state of purity, whether alive or dead, Muslim or disbeliever.

Core Idea
A human spoils the well only if impure or dead without ghusl; a living pure Muslim does not.

This lesson examines the detailed rulings for when a human falls into a well. The ruling depends on multiple factors: whether the person is alive or dead, Muslim or disbeliever, in a state of purity or impurity, and whether they have performed istinja' or ghusl. The author classifies these scenarios to determine when the well water becomes impure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the human's state
Determine if the human is alive or dead, Muslim or disbeliever, and their state of purity (real or ritual impurity, istinja', ghusl).
Alive includes the possibility of entering with intention of ghusl or retrieving bucket.
2
Apply ruling for alive human
If alive, pure, and performed istinja' → water not spoiled. If Muslim in janabah/hadath and immerses with intention of ghusl or bucket → ruling preceded (not spoiled). If disbeliever → spoil water (must draw).
Disbeliever's body is not free from real or ruling impurity.
3
Apply ruling for dead human
If Muslim died after ghusl → not spoiled; if before ghusl → spoiled. Disbeliever always spoils, before or after ghusl.
Ghusl here refers to the ritual washing of the deceased.
4
Distinguish real vs. ruling impurity
Real impurity is actual najasah on body; ruling impurity is hadath (janabah, wudu). The ruling may differ based on which type is present.
The text mentions 'real or ruling impurity' for the disbeliever.
5
Note the special case of intention
If a Muslim junub enters the well with intention of ghusl, the water remains pure because the act of purification overrides the impurity.
This is a mercy and shows the legal principle that water is not spoiled by an act of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الواقع في البئر إما نجاسة أو حيوان وحكم النجاسة قد تقدم في قوله وتنزح البئر بوقوع نجس» على ما أسلفناه، والحيوان إما آدمي أو غيره، وغير الآدمي إما نجس العين أو غيره. وغير نجس العين إما مأكول اللحم أو غيره والكل إما أن أخرج حياً أو ميتاً والميت إما منتفخ أو غيره. فالآدمي إذا خرج حياً ولم يكن في بدنه نجاسة حقيقية أو حكمية وكان مستنجياً لم يفسد الماء وإن كان مسلماً جنباً أو محدثاً فانغمس بنية الغسل أو لطلب الدلو فقد تقدم حكمه، وإن كان كافراً روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه ينزح ماؤها لأن بدنه لا يخلو عن نجاسة حقيقة أو حكماً، وإن أخرج ميتاً وكان مسلماً وقع بعد الغسل لم يفسد الماء وإن كان قبله فسد، والكافر يفسد قبل الغسل وبعده وغير الآدمي إن كان نجس العين كالخنزير والكلب على القول بأنه نجس العين نجس البئر، مات أو لم يمت أصاب الماء فمه أو لم يصب. وعلى القول بأن الكلب ليس بنجس العين لا ينجسه إذا لم يصل فمه إلى الماء وهو الأصح ، وقيل دبره منقلب إلى الخارج فلهذا يفسد الماء بخلاف غيره من الحيوانات. وأما سائر الحيوانات فإن علم ببدنه نجاسة تنجس الماء وإن لم يصل فمه إلى الماء، وقيدنا بالعلم لأنهم قالوا في البقر ونحوه يخرج ولا يجب نزح شيء وإن كان الظاهر اشتمال بولها على أفخاذها لكن يحتمل طهارتها بأن سقطت عقب دخولها ماء كثيراً. هذا . مع أن الأصل الطهارة، وإن لم يعلم ولم يصل فمه إلى الماء، فإن كان مما يؤكل لحمه فلا يوجب التنجيس أصلاً، وإن كان مما لا يؤكل لحمه من السباع والطيور ففيه اختلاف المشايخ والأصح عدم التنجيس، وكذلك في الحمار والبغل والصحيح أنه لا يصير الماء مشكوكاً فيه، وقيل ينزح ماء البئر كله وإن وصل لعابه فحكم الماء حكمه طاهر في حق غيره إلا أن يكمل على ما إذا غسل عنه قبل الوقوع في البئر.
Then know that what falls into the well is either impurity or an animal. The ruling for impurity has preceded in his saying 'and the well is drawn from when an impurity falls' as we have mentioned. The animal is either a human or other. The non-human is either inherently impure or not. The non-inherently impure is either edible or not. All are either taken out alive or dead, and the dead is either bloated or not. As for the human: if taken out alive and his body has no real or ruling impurity and he has performed istinja', he does not spoil the water. If he is a Muslim in a state of janabah or hadath and he immerses with the intention of ghusl or to retrieve the bucket, his ruling has preceded. If he is a disbeliever, it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that the water must be drawn because his body is not free from real or ruling impurity. If taken out dead: if he is Muslim and died after ghusl, he does not spoil the water; if before ghusl, he spoils it. The disbeliever spoils it before and after ghusl. As for non-human: if it is inherently impure like a pig or dog according to the view that it is inherently impure, it makes the well impure whether it died or not, whether its mouth touched water or not. According to the view that the dog is not inherently impure, it does not make it impure if its mouth does not reach the water, and this is the most sound. It is said that its anus is turned outward, so it spoils the water unlike other animals. As for all other animals: if impurity is known on its body, it makes the water impure even if its mouth does not reach the water. We restricted with 'if known' because they said regarding cows and similar animals: it comes out and nothing must be drawn, although apparently urine covers its thighs, but it is possible that it is pure because it entered after much water. This is while the original ruling is purity. If it is not known and its mouth did not reach the water: if it is edible meat, it does not cause impurity at all; if it is non-edible meat from predators and birds, there is disagreement among the mashayikh, and the most sound is that it does not cause impurity. Similarly for donkey and mule: the correct view is that the water does not become questionable; it is said that all the water must be drawn. If its saliva reaches the water, the ruling of the water is that it is pure in relation to others except that it should be completed like the case when it is washed before falling into the well.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of what falls into a well and the resulting ruling. The author divides impurities (which were covered earlier) and animals. Animals are classified into humans and non-humans, then non-humans into inherently impure (najis al-ayn) and others, then others into edible meat and non-edible. Each category has specific rulings depending on whether the animal is alive or dead, bloated or not, and whether its mouth touches water. For humans, the ruling depends on state of purity, whether Muslim or disbeliever, and whether deceased after ritual washing. For inherently impure animals like pig and dog, the well becomes impure regardless, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dog: some say it is not inherently impure and only makes the water impure if its mouth touches water. The author notes the most sound view (al-asahh) is that the dog is not inherently impure. For other animals, if bodily impurity is known, the water is impure; if not known and mouth did not touch water, edible animals do not cause impurity, and non-edible ones have  disagreement (khilaf al-mashayikh) but the most sound is no impurity. For donkey and mule, the water does not become questionable; if saliva enters, the water is considered pure but not purifying (ghayr tahur) according to some.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-‘ayn): An animal whose entire body is considered impure by its nature, e.g., pig.
الاستنجاء (istinjā’): Cleaning the private parts after excretion with water or stones.
حقيقة (haqīqa): Real impurity (like urine, blood) on the body.
حكمية (hukmiyya): Ruling impurity, i.e., the state of hadath (need for wudu or ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim who is alive and has performed istinja' falls into a well. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The water becomes impure because any human contact spoils it.
B) The water remains pure because no impurity is present.
C) The water becomes impure only if the person is a disbeliever.
D) The water becomes impure because the person is not circumcised.

Key Takeaway
A pure living human does not spoil well water; only those with actual impurity or disbelievers do.

Inherently Impure Animals and the Dog Dispute
Rulings for inherently impure animals and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on inherently impure animals (pig and dog) and identify the preferred view in the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dog.

Core Idea
The pig always makes water impure; the dog's ruling has two views; the most sound is that it is not inherently impure.

This lesson focuses on animals that are considered inherently impure (najis al-‘ayn), specifically the pig and the dog. While the pig is unanimously regarded as inherently impure in the Hanafi school, there is a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dog. The passage presents both views and identifies the most sound position (al-asahh). Understanding this khilaf is crucial for correctly determining the purity of well water.

Guided Path
1
Define najis al-‘ayn
Understand that 'najis al-‘ayn' refers to an animal whose entire body is considered impure by nature. In this lesson, pig and (according to some) dog fall into this category.
The dog is disputed; the pig is not.
2
State ruling for pig
The pig is inherently impure. If it falls into a well, the water becomes impure regardless of whether it is alive or dead, and whether its mouth touches the water or not.
No dispute on pig.
3
Identify the two views on the dog
First view: dog is najis al-‘ayn → makes water impure in all cases. Second view (preferred): dog is not najis al-‘ayn → only makes water impure if its mouth touches the water.
The author states 'wa huwa al-asahh' (this is the most sound).
4
Analyze the reason given for the second view
The text mentions a rationale: the dog's anus is turned outward (dubruhu munqalibun ila al-kharij), so it does not contaminate unless the mouth touches water.
This is an aqli (rational) argument supporting the dog not being inherently impure.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the dog is not inherently impure; thus, it only spoils water if its mouth contacts the water.
This ruling has practical implications for drawing water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الواقع في البئر إما نجاسة أو حيوان وحكم النجاسة قد تقدم في قوله وتنزح البئر بوقوع نجس» على ما أسلفناه، والحيوان إما آدمي أو غيره، وغير الآدمي إما نجس العين أو غيره. وغير نجس العين إما مأكول اللحم أو غيره والكل إما أن أخرج حياً أو ميتاً والميت إما منتفخ أو غيره. فالآدمي إذا خرج حياً ولم يكن في بدنه نجاسة حقيقية أو حكمية وكان مستنجياً لم يفسد الماء وإن كان مسلماً جنباً أو محدثاً فانغمس بنية الغسل أو لطلب الدلو فقد تقدم حكمه، وإن كان كافراً روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه ينزح ماؤها لأن بدنه لا يخلو عن نجاسة حقيقة أو حكماً، وإن أخرج ميتاً وكان مسلماً وقع بعد الغسل لم يفسد الماء وإن كان قبله فسد، والكافر يفسد قبل الغسل وبعده وغير الآدمي إن كان نجس العين كالخنزير والكلب على القول بأنه نجس العين نجس البئر، مات أو لم يمت أصاب الماء فمه أو لم يصب. وعلى القول بأن الكلب ليس بنجس العين لا ينجسه إذا لم يصل فمه إلى الماء وهو الأصح ، وقيل دبره منقلب إلى الخارج فلهذا يفسد الماء بخلاف غيره من الحيوانات. وأما سائر الحيوانات فإن علم ببدنه نجاسة تنجس الماء وإن لم يصل فمه إلى الماء، وقيدنا بالعلم لأنهم قالوا في البقر ونحوه يخرج ولا يجب نزح شيء وإن كان الظاهر اشتمال بولها على أفخاذها لكن يحتمل طهارتها بأن سقطت عقب دخولها ماء كثيراً. هذا . مع أن الأصل الطهارة، وإن لم يعلم ولم يصل فمه إلى الماء، فإن كان مما يؤكل لحمه فلا يوجب التنجيس أصلاً، وإن كان مما لا يؤكل لحمه من السباع والطيور ففيه اختلاف المشايخ والأصح عدم التنجيس، وكذلك في الحمار والبغل والصحيح أنه لا يصير الماء مشكوكاً فيه، وقيل ينزح ماء البئر كله وإن وصل لعابه فحكم الماء حكمه طاهر في حق غيره إلا أن يكمل على ما إذا غسل عنه قبل الوقوع في البئر.
Then know that what falls into the well is either impurity or an animal. The ruling for impurity has preceded in his saying 'and the well is drawn from when an impurity falls' as we have mentioned. The animal is either a human or other. The non-human is either inherently impure or not. The non-inherently impure is either edible or not. All are either taken out alive or dead, and the dead is either bloated or not. As for the human: if taken out alive and his body has no real or ruling impurity and he has performed istinja', he does not spoil the water. If he is a Muslim in a state of janabah or hadath and he immerses with the intention of ghusl or to retrieve the bucket, his ruling has preceded. If he is a disbeliever, it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that the water must be drawn because his body is not free from real or ruling impurity. If taken out dead: if he is Muslim and died after ghusl, he does not spoil the water; if before ghusl, he spoils it. The disbeliever spoils it before and after ghusl. As for non-human: if it is inherently impure like a pig or dog according to the view that it is inherently impure, it makes the well impure whether it died or not, whether its mouth touched water or not. According to the view that the dog is not inherently impure, it does not make it impure if its mouth does not reach the water, and this is the most sound. It is said that its anus is turned outward, so it spoils the water unlike other animals. As for all other animals: if impurity is known on its body, it makes the water impure even if its mouth does not reach the water. We restricted with 'if known' because they said regarding cows and similar animals: it comes out and nothing must be drawn, although apparently urine covers its thighs, but it is possible that it is pure because it entered after much water. This is while the original ruling is purity. If it is not known and its mouth did not reach the water: if it is edible meat, it does not cause impurity at all; if it is non-edible meat from predators and birds, there is disagreement among the mashayikh, and the most sound is that it does not cause impurity. Similarly for donkey and mule: the correct view is that the water does not become questionable; it is said that all the water must be drawn. If its saliva reaches the water, the ruling of the water is that it is pure in relation to others except that it should be completed like the case when it is washed before falling into the well.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of what falls into a well and the resulting ruling. The author divides impurities (which were covered earlier) and animals. Animals are classified into humans and non-humans, then non-humans into inherently impure (najis al-ayn) and others, then others into edible meat and non-edible. Each category has specific rulings depending on whether the animal is alive or dead, bloated or not, and whether its mouth touches water. For humans, the ruling depends on state of purity, whether Muslim or disbeliever, and whether deceased after ritual washing. For inherently impure animals like pig and dog, the well becomes impure regardless, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dog: some say it is not inherently impure and only makes the water impure if its mouth touches water. The author notes the most sound view (al-asahh) is that the dog is not inherently impure. For other animals, if bodily impurity is known, the water is impure; if not known and mouth did not touch water, edible animals do not cause impurity, and non-edible ones have  disagreement (khilaf al-mashayikh) but the most sound is no impurity. For donkey and mule, the water does not become questionable; if saliva enters, the water is considered pure but not purifying (ghayr tahur) according to some.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-‘ayn): An animal whose entire body is considered impure by its nature, e.g., pig.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most sound view among multiple scholarly opinions.
دَبره منقلب إلى الخارج (dubruhu munqalibun ila al-kharij): Its anus is turned outward, meaning its feces do not touch its body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most sound Hanafi view, when does a dog falling into a well make the water impure?
A) Always, even if it does not touch the water.
B) Only if its mouth touches the water.
C) Only if it is dead.
D) Only if it is alive and bloated.

Key Takeaway
The dog is not inherently impure in the preferred Hanafi view; only mouth contact spoils water.

Other Animals: Edible, Non-Edible, and Saliva Rulings
Rulings for non-inherently impure animals, including saliva effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the ruling for non-inherently impure animals (edible, non-edible, donkey, mule) falling into a well and evaluate the effect of their saliva.

Core Idea
Edible animals never spoil water; non-edible animals may or may not, but the soundest view is they do not.

This lesson covers all remaining animals that are not inherently impure. The category is further divided into edible meat animals and non-edible ones (predators, birds, donkey, mule). The passage provides detailed conditions: whether impurity is known on the body, whether the animal is alive or dead, bloated or not, and whether its mouth touches the water. The most sound ruling is that non-edible animals also do not spoil water unless there is known impurity. The effect of saliva is also discussed.

Guided Path
1
Classify the animal
First determine if the animal is inherently impure (already covered) or not. If not, then determine if it is edible meat (mā'kul al-lahm) or non-edible (ghayr mā'kul).
Examples: cow, sheep (edible); wolf, hawk (non-edible); donkey, mule (non-edible but special).
2
Check for known impurity on the body
If actual impurity (najasah) is known on the animal's body, it makes the water impure regardless of whether the mouth touches water. This applies to all animals.
The text emphasizes 'if known' (bi'l-'ilm).
3
If no known impurity and mouth did not touch water
Edible animals: do not cause impurity at all. Non-edible animals (predators, birds): there is disagreement among mashayikh, but the most sound is that they do not cause impurity.
Donkey and mule: the correct view is that water does not become questionable (mashkuk); it is not impure.
4
If saliva reaches the water
If the animal's saliva (lu‘āb) enters the water, the ruling depends: the water is considered pure in relation to others (tāhir) but not purifying (tahūr) according to some opinions; the text says it should be completed like the case when washed before falling.
This means the water can be used for non-ritual purposes but not for purification.
5
Note the principle of original purity
The author invokes the principle that the original ruling is purity (al-aṣl al-ṭahārah). This supports the lenient rulings for animals where there is doubt.
This is a key usul principle guiding the rulings.

ثم اعلم أن الواقع في البئر إما نجاسة أو حيوان وحكم النجاسة قد تقدم في قوله وتنزح البئر بوقوع نجس» على ما أسلفناه، والحيوان إما آدمي أو غيره، وغير الآدمي إما نجس العين أو غيره. وغير نجس العين إما مأكول اللحم أو غيره والكل إما أن أخرج حياً أو ميتاً والميت إما منتفخ أو غيره. فالآدمي إذا خرج حياً ولم يكن في بدنه نجاسة حقيقية أو حكمية وكان مستنجياً لم يفسد الماء وإن كان مسلماً جنباً أو محدثاً فانغمس بنية الغسل أو لطلب الدلو فقد تقدم حكمه، وإن كان كافراً روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه ينزح ماؤها لأن بدنه لا يخلو عن نجاسة حقيقة أو حكماً، وإن أخرج ميتاً وكان مسلماً وقع بعد الغسل لم يفسد الماء وإن كان قبله فسد، والكافر يفسد قبل الغسل وبعده وغير الآدمي إن كان نجس العين كالخنزير والكلب على القول بأنه نجس العين نجس البئر، مات أو لم يمت أصاب الماء فمه أو لم يصب. وعلى القول بأن الكلب ليس بنجس العين لا ينجسه إذا لم يصل فمه إلى الماء وهو الأصح ، وقيل دبره منقلب إلى الخارج فلهذا يفسد الماء بخلاف غيره من الحيوانات. وأما سائر الحيوانات فإن علم ببدنه نجاسة تنجس الماء وإن لم يصل فمه إلى الماء، وقيدنا بالعلم لأنهم قالوا في البقر ونحوه يخرج ولا يجب نزح شيء وإن كان الظاهر اشتمال بولها على أفخاذها لكن يحتمل طهارتها بأن سقطت عقب دخولها ماء كثيراً. هذا . مع أن الأصل الطهارة، وإن لم يعلم ولم يصل فمه إلى الماء، فإن كان مما يؤكل لحمه فلا يوجب التنجيس أصلاً، وإن كان مما لا يؤكل لحمه من السباع والطيور ففيه اختلاف المشايخ والأصح عدم التنجيس، وكذلك في الحمار والبغل والصحيح أنه لا يصير الماء مشكوكاً فيه، وقيل ينزح ماء البئر كله وإن وصل لعابه فحكم الماء حكمه طاهر في حق غيره إلا أن يكمل على ما إذا غسل عنه قبل الوقوع في البئر.

Key Terms
السباع (al-sibā‘): Predatory animals that hunt with fangs, e.g., wolves, lions.
اللعاب (al-lu‘āb): Saliva or drool from the animal's mouth.
الأصل الطهارة (al-asl al-ṭahārah): The original ruling is that things are pure until proven otherwise.

Checkpoint
A fox (non-edible predator) falls into a well, is removed, and there is no visible impurity and its mouth did not touch water. According to the most sound Hanafi view, what should be done?
A) Draw all the well water, because fox is not edible.
B) No water needs to be drawn; the water remains pure.
C) Draw half the well water as a precaution.
D) The water becomes questionable and cannot be used until tested.

Key Takeaway
Non-edible animals do not spoil well water in the soundest view unless actual impurity is known.

Ruling on Water When Donkey or Mule Saliva Falls In
Grammatical commentary and ruling on water purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of water contaminated by donkey/mule saliva and distinguish between impurity (najis) and non-purifying (ghayr tahur).

Core Idea
Donkey/mule saliva makes water pure but not purifying—like used water, it cannot be used for wudu.

In this short passage, the commentator clarifies a grammatical point from the base text and then discusses a practical issue: the effect of donkey or mule saliva on well water. While some sources suggest drawing all water as an obligation, the preferred Hanafi view, based on Imam Muhammad, is that the water remains pure (tahir) but loses its purifying quality (tahur). This distinction is crucial for knowing when water can be used for ritual purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
What is the scenario? Donkey or mule saliva enters well water. First, understand that the passage discusses the ruling of such water.
The passage also contains a grammatical note, but the core ruling is about the water's legal status.
2
Determine the legal status
According to Imam Muhammad (as cited in al-Muhit), the water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr tahur), similar to used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal). It is not impure (najis).
The condition is that the water is not overcome (ghalaba) by the saliva; if overcome, it becomes impure.
3
Apply the practical consequence
Since the water is not purifying, it cannot be used for wudu or ghusl. However, it can be used for other purposes like washing dishes or clothes. Drawing all water from the well is recommended as a precaution but not obligatory.
Some later fatwas (Fatawa Qadikhan) recommend drawing ten or more buckets, but the mufta bihi is that it is not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بأن سقطت) أي النجاسة وضمير دخولها للبقر وماء بالنصب مفعول دخول قوله : فيجب نزح الجميع) أقول : ليس في عبارة الخانية لفظة «يجب» بل قال ينزح جميع الماء، نعم ظاهره الوجوب ومثل عبارة الخانية عبارة الحاوي القدسي ومنية المصلي وعزاه شارحها ابن أمير حاج إلى البدائع، وكذا في الدرر وعزاه شارحها الشيخ إسمعيل إلى المبتغى قوله : (ينزح منها عشرون (دلواً والعصفورة ونحوها تعادل الفأرة في الجثة فأخذت حكمها، والعشرون بطريق الإيجاب والثلاثون بطريق الاستحباب كذا في الهداية . فيجب نزح الجميع إذا وصل لعاب البغل أو الحمار إلى الماء. كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وغيرها. لكن في المحيط : ولو وقع سؤر الحمار في الماء يجوز التوضؤ به ما لم يغلب عليه لأنه طاهر غير طهور كالماء المستعمل عند محمد ا هـ . وظاهر كلام صاحب الهداية في التنجيس أن معنى قولهم يجب نزح الجميع أنه لا لأجل النجاسة بل لأنه كان غير طهور، ولا يجب النزاح إذا وقع في البئر ما يكره سؤره ووصل لعابه إلى الماء، لكن في فتاوى قاضيخان ينزح منها دلاء عشرة أو أكثر احتياطاً وثقة .
His saying: 'by her falling' i.e., the impurity, and the pronoun of 'her entering' refers to the cow, and 'water' is in the accusative as object of 'entering'. His saying: 'so it is obligatory to draw all'. I say: there is no word 'obligatory' in the phrase of al-Khaniyyah, but he said: 'he draws all the water', yes its apparent meaning is obligation. Similar to the phrase of al-Khaniyyah is the phrase of al-Hawi al-Qudsi and Munyat al-Musalli, and its commentator Ibn Amir Haj attributed it to al-Bada'i'. Likewise in al-Durar and its commentator Shaykh Isma'il attributed it to al-Mubtagha. His saying: 'twenty buckets are drawn from it, and the sparrow and similar ones equal the mouse in body so they take its ruling, and twenty is by way of obligation and thirty by way of recommendation, as in al-Hidayah'. So it is obligatory to draw all if the saliva of the mule or donkey reaches the water. Similarly in Fatawa Qadikhan and others. However, in al-Muhit: 'if the leftover water of a donkey falls into water, it is permissible to perform wudu with it as long as it is not overcome, because it is pure but not purifying, like used water according to Muhammad.' And the apparent meaning of the author of al-Hidayah in al-Tanjis is that the meaning of their statement 'it is obligatory to draw all' is not because of impurity, but because it is not purifying. And it is not obligatory to draw if something whose leftover is disliked falls into the well and its saliva reaches the water, but in Fatawa Qadikhan, ten or more buckets are drawn from it as a precaution and for confidence.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage first provides a grammatical commentary on the phrase 'bihan saqatat' from the base text, explaining that 'impurity' is the subject of falling and the pronoun refers to the cow. It then addresses the obligation of drawing all water when donkey or mule saliva enters the well. The author notes that al-Khaniyyah does not explicitly say 'obligatory', but the apparent meaning is obligation. He clarifies that according to al-Hidayah, drawing all water is required not because the water becomes impure (najis), but because it loses its purifying quality (tahur) and becomes like used water (musta'mal). Thus, it is pure but not purifying. However, Fatawa Qadikhan recommends drawing ten or more buckets as a precaution. The author concludes that if something with disliked leftover falls in, drawing all is not obligatory but recommended.

Key Terms
سؤر (su’r): The leftover water or saliva of an animal.
طهور (tahūr): Water that is both pure and purifying (can be used for wudu and ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad (as cited in al-Muhit), what is the ruling of water into which a donkey's leftover (su’r) has fallen?
A) It becomes impure (najis) and cannot be used.
B) It remains pure and purifying (tahur).
C) It is pure but not purifying (tahir), like used water.
D) It becomes disliked (makruh) for wudu.

Key Takeaway
Donkey or mule saliva does not make well water impure; it only strips it of its purifying quality, so it cannot be used for wudu.

The Obligatory and Recommended Buckets for a Mouse in a Well
Ruling on drawing buckets for a dead mouse, with evidence from narration and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drawing buckets from a well when a mouse dies in it and identify the evidence from the narration of Anas (RA).

Core Idea
For a mouse, 20 buckets are obligatory, 30 recommended — the 'or' signals certainty vs. preserving the transmission.

This lesson examines the core ruling in the Hanafi school regarding a mouse found dead in a well. Based on a Prophetic narration, the scholars derived an obligatory minimum (20 buckets) and a recommended additional amount (30 buckets) to harmonize between textual fidelity and legal certainty. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of purification (taharah) in wells.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-tier classification
In Zahir al-Riwayah, the ruling for dead animals in a well is on three levels: for a mouse 20 or 30 buckets, for a chicken 40-60, and for a sheep the entire well. Focus on the mouse case.
These amounts apply when the animal died and is not bloated or disintegrated.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The narration from Anas (RA) states: 'For a mouse that died in a well and was taken out immediately, twenty or thirty buckets are drawn.' This is the main textual proof.
The chain goes through Abu Ali al-Samarqandi.
3
Interpret the particle 'or' (أو)
The 'or' indicates two possibilities. The lesser (20) is certain and thus obligatory; the greater (30) is added to preserve the transmitted wording but is not required for purification, hence recommended.
This is the first of two meanings mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam.
4
Understand the second rationale: conflicting reports
Since narrations vary (3,7,20,30,40), scholars chose 20 as the middle between the extremes (3 and 40), making it obligatory and anything beyond recommended.
This second meaning also supports 20 as the wajib amount.
5
Apply the practical ruling
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is: drawing 20 buckets is obligatory (wajib); drawing 30 is recommended (mustahabb). The well becomes pure after 20 buckets.
This is the position of the majority, including the author of al-Hidayah and al-Quduri.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التبيين : يستحب نزح الماء كله ولا يخفى مافيه. وهذا كله إذا خرج حياً، فإن مات وانتفخ أو تفسخ فالواجب نزح الجميع في الجميع، وإن لم ينتفخ ولم يتفسخ فالمذكور في ظاهر الرواية أنه على ثلاث مراتب كما دل عليه كلام المصنف والقدوري وصاحب الهداية وغيرهم. ففي الفأرة ونحوها عشرون أو ثلاثون، وفي الدجاجة ونحوها أربعون أو خمسون أو ستون، وفي الشاة ونحوها ينزح ماء البئر كله وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة جعله على خمس مراتب ففي الحلمة واحد الحلم وهي القراد الضخم العظيم والفأرة الصغيرة عشر دلاء، وفي الفأرة الكبيرة عشرون، وفي الحمامة ثلاثون، وفي الدجاجة أربعون، وفي الآدمي ماء البئر كله وقد قدمنا أن مسائل الآبار مبنية على اتباع الآثار فذكر مشايخنا في كتبهم آثاراً : الأول عن أنس رضي الله عنه أنه قال في الفأرة ماتت في البئر وأخرجت من ساعتها ينزح منها عشرون دلواً. الثاني عن أبي سعيد الخدري أنه قال في الدجاجة إذا ماتت في البئر ينزح منها أربعون دلواً. قال في الغاية لم يذكر أحد من أهل الحديث فيما علمته حديث أنس بن رضي قال في النهاية : وهذا الوضع لمعنيين ذكرهما شيخ الإسلام في مبسوطة : أحدهما أن السنة جاءت في رواية أنس مالك الله تعالى عنه عن النبي أنه قال في الفأرة إذا وقعت في البئر فماتت فيها ينزح منها عشرون دلواً أو ثلاثون هكذا رواه أبو علي السمرقندي بإسناده، وأو لأحد الشيئين وكان الأقل ثابتاً بيقين وهو معنى الوجوب والأكثر يؤتى به لئلا يترك اللفظ المروي وإن كان مستغنى عنه في العمل وهو معنى الاستحباب والثاني أن الرواة اختلفت فيها اختلافاً كثيراً فروى ميسرة عن علي بن أبي طالب في الفأرة تموت في البئر ينزح منها دلاء، وفي رواية سبع دلاء، وفي رواية عشرون، وفي رواية ثلاثون، وروي عن ابن عباس في الفأرة أربعون فإذا بعضهم أوجب في الفأرة عشرين، وبعضهم أقل من عشرين وبعضهم أكثر من عشرين فأخذ علماؤنا بالعشرين لأنه الأسوط بين القليل والكثير فكان هو واجباً لتعينه وما وراءه استحباباً واعترض صاحب النهاية على المعنى الثاني حيث قال : فيه نظر لأن هذا المعنى موجود في الثلاثين فلم يتعين عشرون للوجوب اهـ. يقول الفقير : هذا النظر ساقط لأن وجود هذا المعنى في ثلاثين ممنوع بل الثلاثون إنما هو الوسط بين الأوسط والأكثر لا بين القليل والكثير فإن الروايات الواردة في الفأرة خمس: أحدها دلاء بدون التعيين فهي محمولة على الأقل المتيقن من صيغة الجمع وهو الثلاث، والثانية سبع والثالثة وعشرون،
And in al-Tabyin: it is recommended to draw all the water, and what is in it is not hidden. All this if [the animal] came out alive. If it died and was bloated or disintegrated, then it is obligatory to draw all in all cases. If it was not bloated and did not disintegrate, then what is mentioned in the apparent riwayah is that it is on three levels, as indicated by the words of the author, al-Quduri, the author of al-Hidayah and others. For a mouse and similar: twenty or thirty. For a chicken and similar: forty or fifty or sixty. For a sheep and similar: the entire well water is drawn. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he made it on five levels: for a small tick (al-halamah) – which is a large tick – and a small mouse: ten buckets; for a large mouse: twenty; for a pigeon: thirty; for a chicken: forty; for a human: all the well water. And we have mentioned that issues of wells are based on following traditions (athar). So our mashayikh mentioned in their books traditions: first from Anas (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said regarding a mouse that died in the well and was taken out immediately: twenty buckets are drawn. Second from Abu Sa‘id al-Khudri that he said regarding a chicken if it dies in the well: forty buckets are drawn. He said in al-Ghayyah: no one among the hadith scholars, as far as I know, mentioned the hadith of Anas. He said in al-Nihayah: And this placement is for two meanings mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam in his Mabsut: one is that the Sunnah came in the narration of Anas from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said regarding a mouse that falls into a well and dies: twenty or thirty buckets are drawn. Thus Abu ‘Ali al-Samarqandi narrated it with his chain, and 'or' indicates one of two things. The lesser is certain, which is the meaning of obligation; the greater is used so that the transmitted wording is not abandoned, although it is not needed in practice, and this is the meaning of recommendation. The second meaning is that the narrators differed greatly. Misrah narrated from ‘Ali ibn Abi Talib regarding a mouse dying in the well: some buckets; in one narration: seven buckets; in another: twenty; in another: thirty; and from Ibn ‘Abbas: forty. So some made twenty obligatory, some less than twenty, some more than twenty. Our scholars took twenty because it is the middle between the few and the many, so it became obligatory by specification, and what is beyond it is recommended. The author of al-Nihayah objected to the second meaning, saying: there is consideration in this because this meaning exists in thirty, so twenty is not specific for obligation. The humble one says: this objection is invalid because the existence of this meaning in thirty is denied; rather, thirty is only the middle between the middle and the many, not between the few and the many. For the narrations about the mouse are five: one: buckets without specification, which is interpreted as the minimum number established from the plural form, which is three; the second: seven; the third: twenty;
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage discusses the exact number of buckets that must be drawn from a well when a dead animal is found, distinguishing between obligatory and recommended amounts. The author presents the three-tier classification from the apparent riwayah: mouse (20 or 30), chicken (40, 50, or 60), sheep (whole well). Then he mentions al-Hasan’s narration from Abu Hanifa with five tiers. Since well issues are based on traditions, the author examines the narration of Anas (RA) about the mouse: twenty or thirty buckets. He explains that the 'or' indicates two possibilities: the lesser (20) is obligatory (certain), the greater (30) is recommended to act upon the transmitted wording. Another interpretation: due to conflicting narrations, scholars chose 20 as the middle between the few and many, making it obligatory. The objection from the author of al-Nihayah that 30 could also be the middle is refuted: the narrations include 3, 7, 20, 30, 40; 20 is the middle between the extremes (3 and 40), whereas 30 is between 20 and 40 (middle of the upper half). Thus, 20 is the obligatory amount, and anything beyond is recommended.

Key Terms
دلو (dalw): A bucket used for drawing water from a well; a unit of measurement.
الاستحباب (al-istihbāb): Recommendation; an action that is rewarded but not obligatory.
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The apparent/well-known transmission from the early Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling for drawing water from a well when a mouse dies in it (not bloated)?
A) 20 buckets are mustahabb, 30 are wajib.
B) 20 buckets are wajib, 30 are mustahabb.
C) 40 buckets are wajib.
D) The entire well must be emptied.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation for a dead mouse in a well is 20 buckets; 30 is recommended, based on the hadith and the principle of taking the certain minimum.

Taharah — Purification

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and the Refutation of al-Nihayah
Analysis of the second meaning for the 20/30 ruling and the refutation of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for choosing 20 buckets over 30 and evaluate the objection from the author of al-Nihayah.

Core Idea
20 is wajib because it is the middle between the few and many; 30 is not the middle — thus the objection fails.

This lesson delves into the second meaning behind the 20/30 distinction: the existence of widely conflicting narrations (3,7,20,30,40). The Hanafi scholars argued that 20 is the numerical midpoint, giving it priority for obligation. The author of al-Nihayah objected that 30 could also be seen as the middle, prompting a decisive refutation from the author of al-Bahr al-Ra’iq. Understanding this argument sharpens one's appreciation of how Hanafi usul handles contradictory reports.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conflicting reports about the mouse
The passage lists five variant narrations: unspecified 'some buckets' (interpreted as 3, the minimum of plural), 7, 20, 30, and 40. The Hanafi scholars had to choose one reliable amount.
The number 3 is derived from the Arabic plural form without specification.
2
Understand the reasoning for choosing 20
The scholars argued that 20 is the arithmetic middle between the extremes: 3 (lowest) and 40 (highest). This middle is given priority because it balances the conflicting reports and is more likely to be correct.
This is the second meaning mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam.
3
Analyze the objection from al-Nihayah
The author of al-Nihayah claimed that 30 could also be the middle (between 20 and 40), so why not make 30 obligatory? He argued the reasoning applies equally.
This challenges the specificity of 20.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra’iq refutes: 30 is the middle between 20 and 40, not between the overall extremes (3 and 40). The true middle of all reports is 20. Therefore, 20 is uniquely positioned as the central value.
The response treats the full range of narrations, not just the upper half.
5
Confirm the practical consequence
The mufta bihi remains 20 wajib, 30 mustahabb. The objection does not overturn the ruling because it is based on an incomplete view of the data.
This shows the importance of considering all reports in istidlal.

Commentary
This passage discusses the exact number of buckets that must be drawn from a well when a dead animal is found, distinguishing between obligatory and recommended amounts. The author presents the three-tier classification from the apparent riwayah: mouse (20 or 30), chicken (40, 50, or 60), sheep (whole well). Then he mentions al-Hasan’s narration from Abu Hanifa with five tiers. He explains that the 'or' indicates two possibilities: the lesser (20) is obligatory (certain), the greater (30) is recommended to act upon the transmitted wording. Another interpretation: due to conflicting narrations, scholars chose 20 as the middle between the few and many, making it obligatory. Key Terms
الأسوط (al-aswaṭ): The middle; the median value.
صاحب النهاية (Ṣāḥib al-Nihāyah): The author of the commentary al-Nihayah fi Ghareeb al-Hadith, a reference work on rare hadith terms; likely Imam al-Ghazali (?) or another. In this context, it refers to a commentator who objected.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling of 20 buckets as wajib relies not only on the wording of the hadith but also on a careful analysis of all conflicting reports, with 20 being the median value.

Three Categories of Well-Water Purification
Ruling with proof: three categories of well purification and their hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi categories for drawing water from a well when an animal dies, and explain the evidence from the hadith of Anas and Abu Sa'id al-Khudri.

Core Idea
Three categories: mouse (20–30 buckets), chicken (40–60), and sheep (all water) — based on transmitted reports.

This lesson presents the core Hanafi classification for wajib water drawing when an animal dies in a well. The ruling distinguishes between decomposed and non-decomposed animals and bases the amounts on specific hadiths from the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
If the saliva of a mule or donkey reaches the water, all water must be drawn, but not because of impurity; it loses its purifying property.
2
Differentiate between alive and dead
If the animal falls alive and its leftover water is makruh, no drawing is obligatory. Only if it dies does the ruling apply.
If it dies and decomposes, all water is wajib.
3
Classify decomposition
If the animal does not decompose, apply the three-category system.
4
Memorize the three categories
Mouse: 20–30 buckets; Chicken: 40–60 buckets; Sheep: all water.
The lesser number is wajib, the greater is recommended.
5
Cite the evidence
The hadith of Anas (mouse: 20 or 30 buckets) and Abu Sa'id (chicken: 40 buckets) are the primary proofs.
The school bases these rulings on transmitted reports, not qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

محمد ا هـ . وظاهر كلام صاحب الهداية في التنجيس أن معنى قولهم يجب نزح الجميع أنه لا لأجل النجاسة بل لأنه كان غير طهور، ولا يجب النزاح إذا وقع في البئر ما يكره سؤره ووصل لعابه إلى الماء، لكن في فتاوى قاضيخان ينزح منها دلاء عشرة أو أكثر احتياطاً وثقة . وفي التبيين : يستحب نزح الماء كله ولا يخفى مافيه. وهذا كله إذا خرج حياً، فإن مات وانتفخ أو تفسخ فالواجب نزح الجميع في الجميع، وإن لم ينتفخ ولم يتفسخ وفي الآدمي ماء البئر كله وقد قدمنا أن مسائل الآبار مبنية على اتباع الآثار فذكر مشايخنا في كتبهم آثاراً : الأول عن أنس رضي الله عنه أنه قال في الفأرة ماتت في البئر وأخرجت من ساعتها ينزح منها عشرون دلواً. الثاني عن أبي سعيد الخدري أنه قال في الدجاجة إذا ماتت في البئر ينزح منها أربعون دلواً. قال في الغاية لم يذكر أحد من أهل الحديث فيما علمته حديث أنس قال في النهاية : وهذا الوضع لمعنيين ذكرهما شيخ الإسلام في مبسوطة : أحدهما أن السنة جاءت في رواية أنس مالك الله تعالى عنه عن النبي أنه قال في الفأرة إذا وقعت في البئر فماتت فيها ينزح منها عشرون دلواً أو ثلاثون هكذا رواه أبو علي السمرقندي بإسناده، وأو لأحد الشيئين وكان الأقل ثابتاً بيقين وهو معنى الوجوب والأكثر يؤتى به لئلا يترك اللفظ المروي وإن كان مستغنى عنه في العمل وهو معنى الاستحباب والثاني أن الرواة اختلفت فيها اختلافاً كثيراً فروى ميسرة عن علي بن أبي طالب في الفأرة تموت في البئر ينزح منها دلاء، وفي رواية سبع دلاء، وفي رواية عشرون، وفي رواية ثلاثون، وروي عن ابن عباس في الفأرة أربعون فإذا بعضهم أوجب في الفأرة عشرين، وبعضهم أقل من عشرين It is obligatory to draw all water if the saliva of a mule or donkey reaches the water. Thus in Fatawa Qadikhan and others. But in al-Muhit: If the leftover saliva of a donkey falls into water, it is permissible to perform wudu with it as long as it does not overcome it because it is pure but not purifying like used water according to Muhammad. The apparent statement of the author of al-Hidayah in al-Tanjis is that the meaning of their statement 'it is obligatory to draw all water' is not because of impurity but because it was not purifying. And it is not obligatory to draw if there falls into the well what is disliked to use its leftover saliva and its saliva reaches the water, but in Fatawa Qadikhan one draws ten or more buckets as a precaution. And in al-Tabyin: it is recommended to draw all the water and what is in it is not hidden. And all this if it came out alive. If it died and inflated or disintegrated, then it is obligatory to draw all in all. And if it did not inflate or disintegrate, then what is mentioned in the apparent narration is that it is on three levels as indicated by the statement of the author and al-Quduri and the author of al-Hidayah and others. So for the mouse and its like, twenty or thirty; for the chicken and its like, forty or fifty or sixty; for the sheep and its like, all the water of the well is drawn. And in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he made it on five levels: for the small tick and the small mouse, ten buckets; for the large mouse, twenty; for the pigeon, thirty; for the chicken, forty; for the human, all the water of the well. And we have mentioned that the issues of wells are based on following traditions, so our scholars mentioned in their books traditions: the first from Anas radiya Allahu 'anhu that he said regarding the mouse that died in the well and was taken out immediately, twenty buckets are drawn. The second from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that he said regarding the chicken if it dies in the well, forty buckets are drawn. He said in al-Ghayah: I do not know of anyone among the hadith scholars who mentioned the hadith of Anas. He said in al-Nihayah: And this placement is for two meanings mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam in his Mabsut: one is that the Sunnah came in the narration of Anas from the Prophet that he said regarding the mouse when it falls in the well and dies, twenty or thirty buckets are drawn, thus narrated by Abu Ali al-Samarqandi with his chain, and 'or' indicates one of two things, and the lesser is established with certainty, which is the meaning of obligation, and the greater is done so as not to leave the narrated wording even if it is dispensed with in practice, which is the meaning of recommendation. And the second is that the narrators differed greatly: Maisarah narrated from Ali ibn Abi Talib regarding the mouse dying in the well, buckets are drawn; in one narration seven buckets; in one narration twenty; in one narration thirty; and it is narrated from Ibn Abbas forty. So some made obligatory twenty, some less than twenty, some more than twenty. So our scholars adopted twenty because it is the middle between the few and the many, so it became obligatory due to its specification, and what is beyond it is recommended. And the author of al-Nihayah objected to the second meaning, saying: There is consideration because this meaning exists in thirty, so twenty is not specified for obligation. The poor one says: This consideration is invalid because the existence of this meaning in thirty is not conceded; rather thirty is the middle between the middle and the many, not between the few and the many. For the narrations regarding the mouse are five: one: buckets without specification, which is carried on the minimum certain from the plural form which is three; second: seven; third: twenty; fourth: thirty; fifth: forty. And it is not hidden from you that twenty among these narrations is the middle between the few and the many because the few is three and seven, and the many is thirty and forty, and twenty is middle between them. Deliberate with true deliberation, you will obtain the result of reflection. End of Fara'id. His saying: (al-Mukhrij) i.e., the author of the book Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah, to distinguish from Imam al-Zayla'i, the commentator of al-Kanz, for he is other than him. His saying: (And it is said that what is considered in every well is its bucket) apparently it is a tafsir of the middle, but it is not so; rather it is opposite to it. He said in al-Bada'i: Then they differed regarding the bucket: some said what is considered in that is the bucket of each well from which it is drawn, whether small or large. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he estimated it as a sa'. And it is said that what is considered is the middle between the small and the large. End. And commentator al-Zayla'i said: The middle is the bucket used in every town, and it is said that what is considered in every well is its bucket because it is easier for them, and it is said that which contains a sa', etc. Contemplate his saying: (In this case, the statement of one who estimated the bucket should be carried...) He said in al-Nahr: I say: The estimation by the sa' is based on the choice that it is the middle; and according to his interpretation of the bucket used in every town, it should be considered for those who lack it as well. So the conclusion is that whoever considers in every well its bucket, the consideration of the middle according to his statement is not feasible except in

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the detailed rulings regarding animals falling into wells and the required amount of water to be drawn (nazh). It is a ruling conveyed with proof (dalil naqli) and contains intra-Hanafi disputes. The commentary follows the order of the text.

The passage begins with the ruling that if the saliva of a mule or donkey reaches the water, all water must be drawn. However, a distinction is made: the reason is not that the water becomes impure (najis), but that it loses its purifying property (tahur), like used water according to Imam Muhammad. Thus, it is not obligatory to draw the water if the animal's leftover water (su'r) is merely disliked (makruh). Nonetheless, as a precaution, some recommend drawing ten or more buckets.

Then comes a key distinction: if an animal falls alive and then dies and decomposes (intafakha or tafassakha), all water must be drawn in all cases. If it does not decompose, the well-known position (zahir al-riwayah) divides cases into three levels: for a mouse and similar small animals, 20 or 30 buckets; for a chicken and similar, 40, 50, or 60; for a sheep and similar, all water. However, in a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad, Abu Hanifa had a five-level classification: a small tick or small mouse (10 buckets), large mouse (20), pigeon (30), chicken (40), human (all water).

The text then explains that rulings on wells are based on transmitted reports (athar). It cites two key athar: from Anas (20 buckets for a mouse) and from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (40 for a chicken). It acknowledges that some hadith scholars did not know of these reports, but the author defends their use by explaining that the Hanafi school relies on them as practiced by earlier scholars.

A significant portion discusses why 20 buckets (not 30) are considered obligatory for a mouse. The author, citing Shaykh al-Islam in al-Mabsut, gives two reasons: (1) In the hadith, the Prophet said "20 or 30," where the lesser number is certain (obligation) and the greater is recommended to preserve the wording. (2) Due to differences in narrations (from 3 to 40 buckets), the Hanafi scholars adopted 20 as the middle ground (aswat) between the least and most, making it obligatory while anything beyond is recommended. An objection that 30 could also be the middle is refuted: the narrations are specifically 3, 7, 20, 30, 40 — so 20 is the middle between the low (3,7) and high (30,40).

Finally, the passage discusses the definition of the bucket (dalw). There is disagreement: some say the bucket used for that particular well (small or large), narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is a sa' (a specific measure), others say the middle-sized bucket. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq suggests that the default is the customary bucket of the locality; if the well has no dedicated bucket, one uses an average bucket. A dispute is noted: Hasan ibn Ziyad and Zufar held that one large bucket is insufficient because the repeated drawing causes water to flow from below, which is not achieved with a single bucket. The preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi) is that a single large bucket suffices if it extracts the required amount.

Throughout, the passage exemplifies the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from transmitted reports, balancing between literal texts and practical convenience.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Drawing water from a well to remove impurity or restore purifying property.
سؤر (su'r): Leftover water or saliva of an animal; its ruling affects the purity of water.
دلو (dalw): A bucket used to draw water; its size is determined by custom or a specific measure like sa'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many buckets must be drawn when a chicken dies in a well without decomposing?
A) 20 buckets
B) 40 buckets
C) 60 buckets
D) All water

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school categorizes well purification into three levels based on transmitted reports, with the specific numbers for mouse, chicken, and sheep.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Rulings on Drawing Water from a Well (Nazh al-Bi'r)
Explains required bucket amounts for well purification after animal death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on well purification after animal death, identify the relied-upon definition of the 'middle bucket', and apply the rule that one large bucket suffices.

Core Idea
To purify a well, remove the required number of buckets by any bucket; continuity is not needed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the details of purifying a well when an animal dies in it. The core issue is determining the required amount of water to be drawn and whether using one large bucket suffices. The Hanafi school provides clear guidelines based on the type of animal and the bucket used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impurity and the required action
Determine that an animal has died in the well, making the water impure. The wajib action is to draw (nazh) a certain number of buckets to remove the impurity.
The number of buckets depends on the animal: e.g., 20 for a mouse, 30 for a chicken, 40 for a pigeon.
2
Understand the dispute on the 'middle bucket' (al-dalw al-wast)
The term 'middle bucket' is interpreted differently: some say it is the bucket commonly used in that city, others say it is the well's own bucket, and some define it by a fixed measure (e.g., 8 ratls). The relied-upon view is to consider the bucket of each well.
This is the choice of al-Hidayah and the apparent riwaya.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on one large bucket
Hasan ibn Ziyad and Zufr argue that one large bucket does not suffice because repeated drawing creates a flow effect. The majority (Abu Hanifa) responds that the maqsud (purpose) is to remove the wajib quantity, not to achieve flow.
The mufta bihi is that one large bucket suffices if it removes the required amount.
4
Examine the question of continuity (tawali)
The passage indicates that continuity in drawing is not required. The well is purified once the wajib quantity has been removed, even if done in separate sessions.
This applies regardless of the bucket size.
5
Apply the ruling to modern contexts
Today, wells are less common but the principle applies to any water source like a cistern or pond. The bucket size is defined by the custom of the locality or the specific water source.
The key is the removal of the wajib quantity; the method of removal is flexible.
