His saying: 'except when its removal is impossible and it is impure' – he excludes the impure substance itself. Al-Qahistani said: In al-Jawahir, if a sparrow falls into a well and they are unable to remove it, as long as it remains inside, it is impure. So they leave it for a time known that it has transformed and become sludge. It is said: a period of six months. End. His saying: 'and reflect on it' refers to the previous from al-Mabsut: 'We are certain that in this well there is only the impurity of a mouse, and the impurity of a mouse is purified by twenty buckets.' In al-Muhit, quoting from al-Nawadir: If a mouse dies in a vessel (habb) and then the water is poured into a well, Muhammad said: Scoop the greater of the poured amount or twenty buckets, and this is the most correct, because if a mouse had fallen into it, twenty would be scooped. Similarly, if what fell into it is poured, unless the poured amount exceeds that, then the excess is scooped with the twenty. Abu Yusuf said: Scoop the poured amount and twenty buckets, because it becomes like two mice falling into the well, requiring their removal and twenty buckets. Similarly, this. In al-Kafi, al-Mustasfa, and al-Bada'i: If a mouse falls into a vessel (habb, with an unpointed ha), all the water is poured out, and no reason is given. The reason is that being satisfied with partial scooping is specific to wells, established by narrations contrary to analogy, so others are not analogized to it. According to this, if a mouse falls into a large reservoir (suhrayj) or a tank (fisqiyya) and they are not ten by ten, then all the water is poured out, as is not hidden. And the well is not considered pure until the last bucket separates from the mouth of the well according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), because the ruling of the bucket is the ruling of what is connected to the water and the well. From Muhammad: It becomes pure upon separation from the water, and the dripping is not considered due to necessity. The fruit of the difference appears when the last bucket separates from the water but not from the mouth of the well, and a man takes some of its water and then returns the bucket. According to them, the water taken before returning is impure; according to him, it is pure. Thus in al-Tabyin. Its apparent meaning is that returning the bucket is a condition, but it is not so. Rather, the water taken before separation from the mouth of the well is impure according to them unconditionally, whether the bucket is returned or not. For this reason, this condition was not mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, Mi'raj al-Diraya, al-Muhit, and many books, so it is superfluous. In al-Bada'i: The saying of Abu Hanifa is not mentioned in the apparent narration; only al-Hakim mentioned it. In al-Tanjis: If impure water is scooped from a well, it is disliked to moisten mud with it and plaster the mosque or its floor with it because of its impurity, unlike dung if placed in mud because there is necessity, as it is not possible otherwise. End. The distance between a cesspool and a well that prevents impurity from reaching the well is five cubits in the narration of Abu Sulayman and seven in the narration of Abu Hafs. He said in his saying 'purifies' etc.: Apparently, by purity he means lightness of impurity, as indicated by what follows. His saying: 'all the water is poured' – apparently it becomes pure by mere pouring without washing. But apparently, it must be washed after pouring three times because it is ruled that the poured water is impure and residue remains, so washing is necessary, unlike a well. His saying: 'according to this, if a mouse falls into the reservoir...' This is only valid based on the fact that a reservoir is not included in the name 'well' in any way. Thus in al-Nahr. He said before: And the implication of their absolute ruling of twenty and forty for the mouse and pigeon is that there is no difference between a bounded (water) and other. Some contemporaries adhered to this and gave a fatwa to scoop twenty for a mouse that falls into a reservoir. In al-Qamus: The reservoir is a large basin where water collects. End. The scholar al-Maqdisi mentioned the author's statement and his inference from al-Kafi and others on the issue of the vessel, then said: It is not hidden that it is far-fetched, for the vessel is a large jar, and where is that compared to a reservoir, especially one that holds thousands of buckets? End. I said: In al-Qunyah, it is narrated that the ruling of a well (rakya) is the ruling of a well (bi'r). Some virtuous said: It is a well as in al-Qamus, but in custom it is a well.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers several connected rulings. First, it discusses the exception for when removing a dead animal from a well is impossible; the water remains impure until the animal decomposes into mud, estimated at six months. Then it moves to the case of a mouse falling into a vessel (habb) and its water poured into a well. Muhammad's ruling is to scoop the greater of the poured amount or 20 buckets, while Abu Yusuf requires scooping both the poured amount and 20. The text then cites al-Kafi et al., stating that for a vessel, all water must be poured out, reasoning that partial scooping is specific to wells by textual evidence (athar) and not analogized to other containers. This leads to the ruling for a reservoir (suhrayj): if not ten by ten cubits, all water is poured out. Next, the text discusses the precise moment a well becomes pure after scooping: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, not until the last bucket separates from the well's mouth; according to Muhammad, separation from the water suffices. A practical difference arises if someone takes water before separation and returns the bucket. Then, a ruling on using the muddy water from purification for plastering: it is disliked except for dung out of necessity. Finally, the minimum distance between a cesspool and a well to prevent contamination is given as 5 or 7 cubits according to different narrations. The passage ends with a discussion on whether a reservoir is treated like a well.

Key Terms
تعذر الإخراج (ta'addhur al-ikhraj): Impossibility of removing the impure object from the well.
استحال (istahala): Transformed into a different substance, e.g., a dead animal turning into mud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for the exception of impossible removal?
A) The animal must be a bird.
B) The removal must be truly impossible.
C) The well must be at least 10x10 cubits.
D) The animal must be alive.

Key Takeaway
When removal is impossible, the well becomes pure only after the impurity transforms into mud, not by time alone.

Mouse in a Vessel Poured into a Well: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on scooping when container water is added to a well.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on scooping when water from a contaminated vessel is poured into a well.

Core Idea
When water from a contaminated vessel is poured into a well, Muhammad says scoop the greater of 20 or the poured amount; Abu Yusuf adds both.

This lesson delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific scenario: a mouse dies in a large jar (habb), and the water is then poured into a well. How much water must be scooped to purify the well? The text presents the positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and also a more stringent view from other Hanafi works requiring all water to be poured out from the vessel initially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A mouse dies in a vessel (habb) of water; the water is then poured into a well. The question is how much to scoop from the well.
This is not a case of direct impurity in the well, but transferred impurity.
2
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani: scoop the greater of the amount poured or 20 buckets. This is the most correct view (al-ashahh).
Reason: if the mouse had fallen directly, 20 buckets would suffice.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf: scoop the poured amount plus 20 buckets, analogizing to two mice falling in.
The poured water is considered an additional impurity.
4
Note the alternative stricter view
Al-Kafi, al-Mustasfa, al-Bada'i: all water from the vessel must be poured out completely, and partial scooping from the well is not acceptable. This is because the concession of partial scooping is specific to wells by textual evidence.
This view is not analogized to wells.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا إذا تعذر إخراجه وكان متنجساً) احترز به عن عين النجاسة قال القهستاني وفي الجواهر لو وقع عصفور في بئر فعجزوا عن إخراجه فما دام فيها فنجسة فتترك مدة يعلم أنه استحال وصار حمأة، وقيل مدة ستة أشهر .اهـ. قوله : (وليتأمل فيه) أي السابقة ما في المبسوط من أنا نتيقن أنه ليس في هذا البئر إلا نجاسة فأرة ونجاسة فأرة يطهرها عشرون دلواً ا وفي المحيط معزياً إلى النوادر : فإن ماتت في حب فأريق الماء في البئر قال محمد : ينزح الأكثر من المصبوب ومن عشرين دلواً وهو الأصح لأن الفارة لو وقعت فيها ينزح عشرون فكذا إذا صب فيها ما وقع فيه إلا إذا زاد المصبوب على ذلك فتنزح الزيادة مع العشرين. وقال أبو يوسف : ينزح المصبوب وعشرون دلواً لأنه . يصير بمنزلة ما لو وقعت الفأرتان في البئر يجب نزحهما ونزح عشرين دلواً كذا هذا. وفي الكافي والمستصفى والبدائع : إن الفأرة إذا وقعت في الحب - بالحاء المهملة - يهراق الماء كله ولم يعلل له. ووجهه أن الاكتفاء بنزح البعض مخصوص بالآبار ثبت بالآثار على خلاف القياس فلا يلحق به غيره، فعلى هذا إذا وقعت الفارة في الصهريج أو الفسقية ولم يكونا عشراً في عشر فإن الماء كله يهراق كما لا يخفى ولا يحكم بطهارة البئر ما لم ينفصل الدلو الأخير عن رأس البئر عندهما لأن حكم الدلو حكم المتصل بالماء والبئر. وعن محمد يطهر بالانفصال عن الماء ولا اعتبار بما يتقاطر للضرورة. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا انفصل الدلو الأخير عن الماء ولم ينفصل عن رأس البئر واستبقى من مائها رجل ثم أعاد الدلو، فعندهما الماء المأخوذ قبل العود نجس، وعنده طاهر. كذا في التبيين وظاهره أن عود الدلو قيد وليس كذلك بل الماء المأخوذ قبل الانفصال عن رأس البئر نجس عندهما مطلقاً، عاد الدلو أو لا، ولهذا لم يذكر هذا القيد في فتح القدير ومعراج الدراية والمحيط وكثير من الكتب فكان زائداً. وفي البدائع لم يذكر في ظاهر الرواية قول أبي حنيفة وإنما ذكره الحاكم. وفي التنجيس : إذا نزح الماء النجس من البئر يكره أن يبل به الطين ويطين به المسجد أو أرضه لنجاسته بخلاف السرقين إذا جعله في الطين لأن في ذلك ضرورة لأنه لا يتهيأ إلا بذلك اهـ . والبعد بين البالوعة والبئر المانع من وصول النجاسة إلى البئر خمسة أذرع في رواية أبي سليمان وسبعة في رواية أبي حفص. وقال في قوله «طهر الخ ) والظاهر أنه أراد بالطهارة خفة النجاسة كما يفيده ما بعده. قوله : (بهراق الماء كله ظاهره أنه يطهر بمجرد الإراقة بلا غسل والظاهر أنه لا بد من غسله بعد الإراقة ثلاثاً لأنه حكم بنجاسة المراق وبقي الأثر فلا بد من غسله بخلاف البئر. قوله : (فعلى هذا إذا وقعت الفأرة في الصهريج الخ هذا إنما يتم بناء على أن الصهريج ليس من مسمى البئر في شيء. كذا في النهر. وقال قبله وقضية إطلاقهم إيجاب العشرين والأربعين في الفأرة والحمامة أنه لا فرق بين المعين وغيرها، وبذلك تمسك بعض أهل العصر وأفتى بنزح عشرين في فأرة وقعت في صهريج . وفي القاموس : الصهريج الحوض الكبير يجتمع فيه الماء .اهـ. وقد ذكر العلامة المقدسي كلام المؤلف واستدلاله بما في الكافي وغيره من مسألة الحب ثم قال : إنه مما لا يخفى بعده فإن الحب بالحاء الخابية وأين هي من الصهريج لا سيما الذي يسع ألوفاً من الدلاء .اهـ. قلت: ونقل في القنية أن حكم الركية حكم البئر. قال بعض الفضلاء وهي البئر كما في القاموس لكن في العرف هي بئر
His saying: 'except when its removal is impossible and it is impure' – he excludes the impure substance itself. Al-Qahistani said: In al-Jawahir, if a sparrow falls into a well and they are unable to remove it, as long as it remains inside, it is impure. So they leave it for a time known that it has transformed and become sludge. It is said: a period of six months. End. His saying: 'and reflect on it' refers to the previous from al-Mabsut: 'We are certain that in this well there is only the impurity of a mouse, and the impurity of a mouse is purified by twenty buckets.' In al-Muhit, quoting from al-Nawadir: If a mouse dies in a vessel (habb) and then the water is poured into a well, Muhammad said: Scoop the greater of the poured amount or twenty buckets, and this is the most correct, because if a mouse had fallen into it, twenty would be scooped. Similarly, if what fell into it is poured, unless the poured amount exceeds that, then the excess is scooped with the twenty. Abu Yusuf said: Scoop the poured amount and twenty buckets, because it becomes like two mice falling into the well, requiring their removal and twenty buckets. Similarly, this. In al-Kafi, al-Mustasfa, and al-Bada'i: If a mouse falls into a vessel (habb, with an unpointed ha), all the water is poured out, and no reason is given. The reason is that being satisfied with partial scooping is specific to wells, established by narrations contrary to analogy, so others are not analogized to it. According to this, if a mouse falls into a large reservoir (suhrayj) or a tank (fisqiyya) and they are not ten by ten, then all the water is poured out, as is not hidden. And the well is not considered pure until the last bucket separates from the mouth of the well according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), because the ruling of the bucket is the ruling of what is connected to the water and the well. From Muhammad: It becomes pure upon separation from the water, and the dripping is not considered due to necessity. The fruit of the difference appears when the last bucket separates from the water but not from the mouth of the well, and a man takes some of its water and then returns the bucket. According to them, the water taken before returning is impure; according to him, it is pure. Thus in al-Tabyin. Its apparent meaning is that returning the bucket is a condition, but it is not so. Rather, the water taken before separation from the mouth of the well is impure according to them unconditionally, whether the bucket is returned or not. For this reason, this condition was not mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, Mi'raj al-Diraya, al-Muhit, and many books, so it is superfluous. In al-Bada'i: The saying of Abu Hanifa is not mentioned in the apparent narration; only al-Hakim mentioned it. In al-Tanjis: If impure water is scooped from a well, it is disliked to moisten mud with it and plaster the mosque or its floor with it because of its impurity, unlike dung if placed in mud because there is necessity, as it is not possible otherwise. End. The distance between a cesspool and a well that prevents impurity from reaching the well is five cubits in the narration of Abu Sulayman and seven in the narration of Abu Hafs. He said in his saying 'purifies' etc.: Apparently, by purity he means lightness of impurity, as indicated by what follows. His saying: 'all the water is poured' – apparently it becomes pure by mere pouring without washing. But apparently, it must be washed after pouring three times because it is ruled that the poured water is impure and residue remains, so washing is necessary, unlike a well. His saying: 'according to this, if a mouse falls into the reservoir...' This is only valid based on the fact that a reservoir is not included in the name 'well' in any way. Thus in al-Nahr. He said before: And the implication of their absolute ruling of twenty and forty for the mouse and pigeon is that there is no difference between a bounded (water) and other. Some contemporaries adhered to this and gave a fatwa to scoop twenty for a mouse that falls into a reservoir. In al-Qamus: The reservoir is a large basin where water collects. End. The scholar al-Maqdisi mentioned the author's statement and his inference from al-Kafi and others on the issue of the vessel, then said: It is not hidden that it is far-fetched, for the vessel is a large jar, and where is that compared to a reservoir, especially one that holds thousands of buckets? End. I said: In al-Qunyah, it is narrated that the ruling of a well (rakya) is the ruling of a well (bi'r). Some virtuous said: It is a well as in al-Qamus, but in custom it is a well.

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers several connected rulings. First, it discusses the exception for when removing a dead animal from a well is impossible; the water remains impure until the animal decomposes into mud, estimated at six months. Then it moves to the case of a mouse falling into a vessel (habb) and its water poured into a well. Key Terms
الحب (al-habb): A large earthen jar or vessel for water storage.
الأصح (al-ashahh): The most correct view among multiple Hanafi opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, if 10 buckets of water from a contaminated vessel are poured into a well, how many buckets must be scooped?
A) 10
B) 20
C) 30
D) All water from the vessel must be discarded

Key Takeaway

Reservoirs and Wells: Scope of the Partial Scooping Ruling
Explains that the partial scooping rule for wells does not extend to reservoirs or other containers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a well (bi'r) and a reservoir (suhrayj) for the purpose of purification, and explain why the partial scooping rule does not apply to reservoirs.

Core Idea
The concession of partial scooping is specific to wells by textual proof, so reservoirs and other containers require removing all water.

This lesson examines the boundary between wells and other water containers. The text clarifies that the ruling allowing partial scooping (e.g., 20 buckets for a mouse) is specific to wells as established by narrations (athar). For reservoirs (suhrayj), tanks (fisqiyya), and other structures, if an impurity falls in, all water must be poured out. This distinction is crucial because it affects how much water must be discarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key ruling
The partial scooping ruling (nazh al-ba'd) is specific to wells. This is established by narrations that are contrary to analogy (khilaf al-qiyas).
2
Define a reservoir (suhrayj)
A reservoir is a large basin for collecting water, often man-made. It is not considered a well in Hanafi fiqh.
Al-Qamus defines it as a large basin.
3
Apply the ruling
If a mouse falls into a reservoir, not a well, all the water must be poured out. The partial scooping concession does not apply.
Exception: if the reservoir is ten by ten cubits or more, it might be considered a large body of water (like a pool) with different rulings. The text says 'if they are not ten in ten'.
4
Note the debate
Some later Hanafis gave fatwa to scoop 20 buckets for a mouse in a reservoir, but the text seems to criticize this, stating it is far-fetched.
Al-Maqdisi criticizes the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the well is not considered pure until the last bucket separates from the mouth of the well according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), because the ruling of the bucket is the ruling of what is connected to the water and the well. From Muhammad: It becomes pure upon separation from the water, and the dripping is not considered due to necessity. The fruit of the difference appears when the last bucket separates from the water but not from the mouth of the well, and a man takes some of its water and then returns the bucket. According to them, the water taken before returning is impure; according to him, it is pure. Thus in al-Tabyin. Its apparent meaning is that returning the bucket is a condition, but it is not so. Rather, the water taken before separation from the mouth of the well is impure according to them unconditionally, whether the bucket is returned or not. For this reason, this condition was not mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, Mi'raj al-Diraya, al-Muhit, and many books, so it is superfluous. In al-Bada'i: The saying of Abu Hanifa is not mentioned in the apparent narration; only al-Hakim mentioned it. In al-Tanjis: If impure water is scooped from a well, it is disliked to moisten mud with it and plaster the mosque or its floor with it because of its impurity, unlike dung if placed in mud because there is necessity, as it is not possible otherwise. End. The distance between a cesspool and a well that prevents impurity from reaching the well is five cubits in the narration of Abu Sulayman and seven in the narration of Abu Hafs. He said in his saying 'purifies' etc.: Apparently, by purity he means lightness of impurity, as indicated by what follows. His saying: 'all the water is poured' – apparently it becomes pure by mere pouring without washing. But apparently, it must be washed after pouring three times because it is ruled that the poured water is impure and residue remains, so washing is necessary, unlike a well. His saying: 'according to this, if a mouse falls into the reservoir...' This is only valid based on the fact that a reservoir is not included in the name 'well' in any way. Thus in al-Nahr. He said before: And the implication of their absolute ruling of twenty and forty for the mouse and pigeon is that there is no difference between a bounded (water) and other. Some contemporaries adhered to this and gave a fatwa to scoop twenty for a mouse that falls into a reservoir. In al-Qamus: The reservoir is a large basin where water collects. End. The scholar al-Maqdisi mentioned the author's statement and his inference from al-Kafi and others on the issue of the vessel, then said: It is not hidden that it is far-fetched, for the vessel is a large jar, and where is that compared to a reservoir, especially one that holds thousands of buckets? End. I said: In al-Qunyah, it is narrated that the ruling of a well (rakya) is the ruling of a well (bi'r). Some virtuous said: It is a well as in al-Qamus, but in custom it is a well.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers several connected rulings. First, it discusses the exception for when removing a dead animal from a well is impossible; the water remains impure until the animal decomposes into mud, estimated at six months. Then it moves to the case of a mouse falling into a vessel (habb) and its water poured into a well. Muhammad's ruling is to scoop the greater of the poured amount or 20 buckets, while Abu Yusuf requires scooping both the poured amount and 20. The text then cites al-Kafi et al., stating that for a vessel, all water must be poured out, reasoning that partial scooping is specific to wells by textual evidence (athar) and not analogized to other containers. This leads to the ruling for a reservoir (suhrayj): if not ten by ten cubits, all water is poured out. Next, the text discusses the precise moment a well becomes pure after scooping: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, not until the last bucket separates from the well's mouth; according to Muhammad, separation from the water suffices. A practical difference arises if someone takes water before separation and returns the bucket. Then, a ruling on using the muddy water from purification for plastering: it is disliked except for dung out of necessity. Finally, the minimum distance between a cesspool and a well to prevent contamination is given as 5 or 7 cubits according to different narrations. The passage ends with a discussion on whether a reservoir is treated like a well.

Key Terms
الصهريج (al-suhrayj): Large basin for water collection.
البالوعة (al-balū'ah): Cesspool or drain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a reservoir (suhrayj) not get the partial scooping rule?
A) Because it is too big
B) Because the partial scooping rule is textually specific to wells
C) Because reservoirs are made of different materials
D) Because the impurity does not spread in reservoirs

Key Takeaway
Partial scooping is a concession unique to wells; it does not apply to reservoirs or other water containers.

Purifying a Well from a Rat: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Additional Contamination
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well purification when a rat's contaminated water is added.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on purifying a well after a rat falls in, and distinguish the positions of Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and the 'complete emptying' view when contaminated water is poured into the well.

Core Idea
A well is purified from a rat by drawing twenty buckets; but when contaminated water is added, scholars differ on whether to add more or to empty all.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on the purification of a well when it becomes contaminated by a rat, either directly or through the addition of water from a contaminated jar. The base ruling from al-Mabsut establishes twenty buckets for a rat, but the scenario of a poured jar leads to three distinct opinions within the school. Understanding this dispute reveals how analogy and textual exception interact in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
From al-Mabsut, the established ruling is that a single rat requires drawing twenty buckets of water from the well. This is the starting point.
The passage states: 'نجاسة فأرة يطهرها عشرون دلواً'.
2
Understand the new scenario
A rat dies in a jar (ḥabb), and the entire jar's water is poured into the well. This introduces additional contamination beyond the rat itself.
The jar water is itself impure due to the rat's corpse inside it.
3
Compare the three views
Muhammad: draw the greater of the poured water and twenty buckets. Abu Yusuf: draw the poured water plus twenty buckets. Some (al-Kafi, etc.): empty all water completely.
Muhammad's view is described as 'al-asahh' (soundest) in the text.
4
Analyze the analogical reasoning
Muhammad analogizes to a single rat (20 buckets base); if poured water exceeds 20, the excess is added. Abu Yusuf analogizes to two rats (40 buckets). The complete emptying view rejects analogy altogether, holding that wells are exceptions to qiyas.
The complete emptying view relies on the principle that wells are purified only by transmitted rulings (āthār), not qiyas.
5
Note the tarjih (preference)
The text explicitly prefers Muhammad's view ('wa huwa al-aṣahh'). This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the later Hanafi tradition.
The tarjih authority is the commentator of al-Baḥr al-Rā'iq, without naming a specific scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابقة ما في المبسوط من أنا نتيقن أنه ليس في هذا البئر إلا نجاسة فأرة ونجاسة فأرة يطهرها عشرون دلواً ا وفي المحيط معزياً إلى النوادر : فإن ماتت في حب فأريق الماء في البئر قال محمد : ينزح الأكثر من المصبوبة ومن عشرين دلواً وهو الأصح لأن الفارة لو وقعت فيها ينزح عشرون فكذا إذا صب فيها ما وقع فيه إلا إذا زاد المصبوب على ذلك فتنزح الزيادة مع العشرين. وقال أبو يوسف : ينزح المصبوب وعشرون دلواً لأنه يصير بمنزلة ما لو وقعت الفأرتان في البئر يجب نزحهما ونزح عشرين دلواً كذا هذا. وفي الكافي والمستصفى والبدائع : إن الفأرة إذا وقعت في الحب - بالحاء المهملة - يهراق الماء كله ولم يعلل له. ووجهه أن الاكتفاء بنزح البعض مخصوص بالآبار ثبت بالآثار على خلاف القياس فلا يلحق به غيره، فعلى هذا إذا وقعت الفارة في الصهريج أو الفسقية ولم يكونا عشراً في عشر فإن الماء كله يهراق كما لا يخفى
The previous (ruling) in al-Mabsut that we are certain that there is no impurity in this well except the impurity of a rat, and the impurity of a rat is purified by twenty buckets. And in al-Muhit, attributed to al-Nawadir: if it died in a jar and the water is poured into the well, Muhammad said: he draws the greater of the poured water and twenty buckets, and this is the soundest opinion, because if a rat falls into (the well), twenty buckets are drawn, so likewise if what fell into it is poured, except if the poured amount exceeds that, then the excess is drawn with the twenty. Abu Yusuf said: he draws the poured water and twenty buckets, because it becomes like two rats falling into the well, requiring their removal and twenty buckets. In al-Kafi, al-Mustasfa, and al-Bada'i': if a rat falls into a jar (with an important letter ha), all the water is emptied, and no explanation is given for it. Its aspect is that the sufficiency of drawing some is specific to wells, established by traditions (athar) contrary to analogy, so nothing else is annexed to it. Accordingly, if a rat falls into a reservoir (sahrij) or a pool (fasqiya) and they are not ten by ten cubits, all the water is emptied, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents multiple positions within the Hanafi school regarding the purification of a well when a rat falls in. The base ruling from al-Mabsut states that a single rat requires drawing twenty buckets. The discussion then extends to the case where a contaminated jar is emptied into the well: Muhammad holds that the majority of the combined water must be drawn, comparing it to a single rat adding to the well; Abu Yusuf analogizes it to two rats requiring forty buckets. The view from al-Kafi and others completely rejects analogy for wells, holding that wells are exceptional and only the specific transmitted rulings apply, so if a rat falls into a container (habb), all water must be emptied. This is applied to reservoirs and pools that are not of the well's specification. The passage thus shows a difference between expanding the ruling by analogy versus strictly adhering to transmitted exceptions.

Key Terms
نزح (nazḥ): Drawing or removing water from a well for purification purposes.
فأرة (fa'ra): Rat or mouse; its carrion causes impurity requiring specific purification measures.
حب (ḥabb): A large earthen jar used for storing water, distinct from a well.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basic ruling for purifying a well after a single rat falls into it, as stated in al-Mabsut?
A) All water must be emptied.
B) 20 buckets must be drawn.
C) 40 buckets must be drawn.
D) The well becomes impure and can never be purified.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes twenty buckets for a rat in a well, but when contaminated water is added, the majority view (Muhammad) requires drawing the greater of the poured water and twenty buckets.

Purification of the Well: The Last Bucket Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well purification timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Sahibayn and Muhammad regarding the moment a well becomes purified, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to determine the purity of water taken before the bucket leaves the well's head.

Core Idea
The well becomes pure when the bucket leaves the well's head — the return of the bucket is irrelevant.

This lesson examines a precise intra-Hanafi dispute about when a well becomes pure after drawing the last bucket of contaminated water. The disagreement between Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Muhammad alone has practical consequences for anyone using the well water. Understanding the moment of purification is essential for ensuring that water used for wudu or ghusl is truly pure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The disputed ruling is the exact moment the well becomes pure after drawing the last bucket of impure water. The act is the purification of the well; the condition is the separation of the bucket.
The dispute is about the condition (shart), not the ruling itself.
2
State the Sahibayn position and their dalil
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), the well does not become pure until the last bucket separates from the well's head (the rim). Their reasoning is that the bucket is considered connected to the water and the well while still inside, so impurity remains until the bucket leaves the well entirely.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
State Muhammad's alternative narration
In a separate narration, Muhammad holds that the well becomes pure as soon as the bucket separates from the water, disregarding any dripping water (due to necessity).
This is a nawadir transmission, not the relied-upon view.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference appears if someone takes water from the bucket after it leaves the water but before it leaves the well's head, and then returns the bucket. According to Sahibayn, that water is impure; according to Muhammad, it is pure.
The author clarifies that the return of the bucket is not a condition; the ruling is absolute.
5
Note the superfluous condition in some texts
Some texts mention 'return of the bucket' as a condition, but the author notes that it is not necessary; the Sahibayn position is absolute. Later works like Fath al-Qadir omit it.
This shows the importance of consulting authoritative sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يحكم بطهارة البئر ما لم ينفصل الدلو الأخير عن رأس البئر عندهما لأن حكم الدلو حكم المتصل بالماء والبئر. وعن محمد يطهر بالانفصال عن الماء ولا اعتبار بما يتقاطر للضرورة. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا انفصل الدلو الأخير عن الماء ولم ينفصل عن رأس البئر واستبقى من مائها رجل ثم أعاد الدلو، فعندهما الماء المأخوذ قبل العود نجس، وعنده طاهر. كذا في التبيين وتظاهر أن عود الدلو قيد وليس كذلك بل الماء المأخوذ قبل الانفصال عن رأس البئر نجس عندهما مطلقاً، عاد الدلو أو لا، ولهذا لم يذكر هذا القيد في فتح القدير ومعراج الدراية والمحيط وكثير من الكتب فكان زائداً. وفي البدائع لم يذكر في ظاهر الرواية قول أبي حنيفة وإنما ذكره الحاكم.
The well is not judged pure until the last bucket separates from the well's head according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the ruling of the bucket is that of a connected thing to the water and the well. From Muhammad: it becomes pure by separation from the water, and no consideration is given to dripping due to necessity. The fruit of the difference appears when the last bucket separates from the water but not from the well's head, and a person takes some of its water, then returns the bucket. According to them, the water taken before return is impure; according to him (Muhammad), it is pure. So in al-Tabyeen. And it appears that the return of the bucket is a condition, but it is not so; rather, the water taken before separation from the well's head is absolutely impure according to them, whether the bucket returns or not, and for this reason this condition is not mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, Miraj al-Diraya, al-Muhit, and many books, so it is superfluous. In al-Bada'i': the opinion of Abu Hanifa is not mentioned in the zahir al-riwaya; al-Hakim mentioned it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage discusses the precise moment when a well becomes purified after drawing the last bucket. The default position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) is that the well is not pure until the bucket leaves the well's head (i.e., the opening of the well), because the bucket is considered connected to the water while in the well. Muhammad, in a separate narration, considers the well pure once the bucket leaves the water, disregarding drips. This difference has practical consequences if someone takes water from the bucket before it leaves the well head: according to the Sahibayn that water is impure, while according to Muhammad it is pure. The author notes that the condition of returning the bucket is not necessary; the ruling is absolute. It also notes that Abu Hanifa's opinion is not recorded in the zahir al-riwaya.

Key Terms
دلو أخير (dalw akhīr): The last bucket drawn from the well during the purification process.
انفصال (infisāl): Separation of the bucket from the water or from the well's head.
رأس البئر (ra's al-bi'r): The top opening of the well, from which the bucket is drawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), when does the well become pure after drawing the last bucket?
A) When the bucket leaves the water surface.
B) When the bucket leaves the well's head (rim).
C) When the bucket is completely emptied.
D) When the next bucket is started.

Key Takeaway
The well's purification depends on the bucket's separation from the well's head, not just from the water, and this ruling is absolute regardless of the bucket's return.

The Criterion for Well Purity Near a Latrine
Ruling on minimum distance between latrine and well, with emphasis on water change criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the distance between a latrine and a well, and apply the criterion of change in water properties to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
The decisive factor is change in water properties, not a fixed distance.

The presence of a latrine near a well raises the concern of impurity seeping into the water. While early narrations suggest specific distances, the Hanafi school later clarified that the actual criterion is whether the water itself shows any sign of contamination. This lesson examines the two transmitted distances and the relied-upon view that supersedes them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the physical issue
The well may be contaminated by seepage from a nearby latrine. The question is: what minimum distance prevents this?
2
Note the two narrated distances
Two narrations exist: 5 cubits (Abu Sulayman) and 7 cubits (Abu Hafs). This shows early scholarly difference.
3
Understand the alternative criterion
Al-Halwani introduced a different criterion: instead of fixed distance, consider whether the water's taste, color, or smell has changed.
This is a shift from a quantitative to a qualitative standard.
4
Recognize the tarjih
Later authoritative works (al-Khulasa, Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Muhit) adopted al-Halwani's criterion as the relied-upon position.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) for fatwa.
5
Apply the criterion
If the water is unchanged, it is permissible regardless of distance; if changed, it is impure even if far away.
The distance narrations are now seen as approximate, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبعد بين البالوعة والبئر المانع من وصول النجاسة إلى البئر خمسة أذرع في رواية أبي سليمان وسبعة في رواية أبي حفص. وقال الحلواني : المعتبر الطعم أو اللون أو الريح فإن لم يتغير جاز وإلا فلا، ولو كان عشرة أذرع . قال في الخلاصة وفتاوى قاضيخان والتعويل عليه، وصححه في المحيط.
The distance between the latrine and the well that prevents impurity from reaching the well is five cubits in the narration of Abu Sulayman and seven in the narration of Abu Hafs. Al-Halwani said: the consideration is the taste, color, or smell; if it does not change, it is permissible, otherwise not, even if it is ten cubits. In al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadikhan, reliance is upon this, and it is corrected in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage gives two narrations regarding the minimum distance required between a latrine (balu'a) and a well to prevent contamination. The authoritative position, however, is that mere distance is not the absolute criterion; what matters is whether the water's properties (taste, color, smell) have changed. If the water remains unchanged, it is permissible even if the distance is less than the narrated distances; if it changes, it is not permissible even if the distance is greater. This is the relied-upon view according to al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadikhan.

Key Terms
بالوعة (bālū'a): Latrine or cesspit where waste collects.
ذراع (dhirā'): Cubit, a unit of length approximately 18-24 inches.
طعم (ta'm): Taste of the water, one of the three properties indicating change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what is the decisive factor in determining whether a well is contaminated by a nearby latrine?
A) Being exactly 5 cubits away.
B) Being exactly 7 cubits away.
C) Change in the water's taste, color, or smell.
D) Both distance and change must be considered.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of well water near a latrine is determined by a change in taste, color, or smell, not by a fixed distance.

Ruling on a Rat Dying in Liquid or Solid Substances
Ruling on impurity from a dead rat in liquids and solids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of liquid vs solid to determine impurity and permissible uses when a rat dies in a non-water substance.

Core Idea
A rat's death in liquid contaminates all; in solid, only the immediate area.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the classical ruling from Al-Mabsut, defining solidity by whether the substance levels immediately after a piece is removed. It also addresses special cases: leather tanned with impure ghee, and the tricky case of vinegar made from wine containing a dead rat. Understanding the liquid-solid distinction is fundamental to many taharah rulings in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Classify the substance
Determine if the substance is liquid (mā'i') or solid (jāmid) using the definition: if the surface levels immediately after removing a portion, it's liquid; otherwise solid.
This definition is from al-Mabsut and applies to all substances (oils, ghee, honey, etc.).
2
Determine impurity scope
If liquid, the entire substance becomes impure (najis). If solid, only the area around the rat is impure; remove the rat and that portion; the rest remains pure.
Hanafi position on liquids: full contamination; solids: local contamination.
3
Identify permissible uses
Impure liquids may still be used for purposes other than the body (e.g., lamp oil, cleaning). Solid substances: the pure portions may be used, but the impure part must be discarded.
Exception: do not use impure oil as lamp fuel in mosques due to prohibition of bringing impurity into the mosque.
4
Apply to buyer's option (khiyār)
If a buyer purchases such a product (e.g., ghee, leather) without knowledge of the impurity, they have the option to return it upon discovering the defect.
This is a standard Hanafi sale contract principle: the buyer has khiyar al-ayb (option of defect).
5
Handle the vinegar case
If a rat dies in wine and the wine becomes vinegar, the preferred opinion is that the vinegar is permissible to drink if the rat did not disintegrate and was removed before the transformation. If the rat remained during the transformation, it is not permissible.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj describes this third opinion as 'ahsan' (best).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ماتت الفأرة في غير الماء فإن كان مائعاً تنجس جميعه وجاز استعماله في غير الأبدان. كذا قالوا. وينبغي أن لا يستصبح به في المساجد لكونه ممنوعاً عن إدخال النجاسة المسجد، وتجوز بيعه وللمشتري الخيار إن لم يعلم به وإن كان جامداً ألقيت الفأرة وما حولها وكان الباقي طاهراً أو جاز الانتفاع بما حولها في غير الأبدان وفي المبسوط وحد الجمود والذوب أنه إذا كان بحال لوقوّر ذلك الموضع لا يستوي من ساعته فهو جامد، وإن كان يستوي من ساعته فهو ذائب . وذكر الاسبيجابي أن الجلد إذا دبغ بذلك السمن يغسل الجلد بالماء ويطهر والمتشرب فيه معفو عنه، ولمن اشتراه الخيار إن لم يعلم به. وفي السراج الوهاج : وإن ماتت الفأرة في الخمر فصار خلاً قال بعضهم : الخل مباح، وقيل لا يحل شربه، وقيل إذا لم تتفسخ فيه جاز وإن تفسخت لم يجز لأنه قد صار فيه جزء ومنها وهذا القول أحسن. وهذا إذا استخرجت منه قبل أن خلاً، أما إذا صار خلاً والفأرة فيه لا يحل شربه، سواء كانت متفسخة أو يصير لا لأنه نجس ا هـ .
If a rat dies in something other than water, then if it is liquid, all of it becomes impure, and it is permissible to use it for other than the body. They said that. It is recommended not to use it as lamp oil in mosques because it is prohibited to bring impurity into the mosque. Its sale is permissible, and the buyer has the option if he is unaware. If it is solid, the rat and what is around it are thrown away, and the rest remains pure, and it is permissible to benefit from what is around it for other than the body. In al-Mabsut, the definition of solidity and solubility: if when a piece is removed from that spot, it does not level immediately, it is solid; if it levels immediately, it is liquid. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that if leather is tanned with that ghee, the leather is washed with water and becomes pure, and what has seeped in is pardoned. The buyer has the option if he is unaware. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if a rat dies in wine and it becomes vinegar, some said the vinegar is permissible; it is said its drinking is not permissible; it is said that if it did not disintegrate, it is permissible, but if it disintegrated, it is not, because it has become part of it. This is the best opinion. This is if it is removed before it becomes vinegar. However, if it becomes vinegar while the rat is in it, its drinking is not permissible, whether disintegrated or not, because it is impure.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings for when a rat dies in a liquid or solid substance. For liquids, the entire substance becomes impure (najis) but can still be used for non-body purposes (e.g., as fuel for lamps, but not in mosques). For solids, only the area around the rat is removed, and the rest remains pure. The text defines solidity by whether the substance levels itself after being scooped. It also addresses special cases: leather tanned with impure ghee can be purified by washing; the buyer has the option to return the item if unaware. The section on vinegar discusses the permissibility of vinegar made from wine that contained a dead rat, with the preferred view that it is permissible if the rat did not disintegrate and was removed before the vinegar process, but not if the rat remained during the transformation.

Key Terms
مائع (mā'i'): Liquid substance; if a rat dies in it, it becomes entirely impure.
جامد (jāmid): Solid substance; rat's impurity only affects the immediate area, the rest remains pure.
خيار (khiyār): Option for the buyer to return a product if a defect is discovered after purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire cheese becomes impure and must be discarded.
B) Only the area around the rat is removed; the rest remains pure.
C) The cheese can be washed and made pure.
D) The cheese becomes pure after the rat is removed, with no need to remove any part.

Key Takeaway
When a rat dies in a non-water substance, the impurity spreads fully in liquids but only locally in solids, and both have limited permissible uses and a buyer's option.

The Criterion for Water Impurity: Change in Properties
Ruling that water becomes impure if taste, color, or smell change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for determining water impurity and apply it to scenarios involving water from wells or tanks.

Core Idea
Water is pure unless its taste, color, or smell changes—quantity is irrelevant in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding water that has come into contact with an impurity. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the sole criterion is a change in the water's properties, not its volume. This ruling is relied upon by later Hanafi authorities and distinguishes the school from others that consider quantity thresholds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is using water that may have contacted an impurity. The ruling: if taste, color, or smell are unchanged, the water is permissible (mubah) to use; if changed, it is impure (najis) and haram to use.
The ruling applies to all uses: drinking, wudu, ghusl, etc.
2
State the criterion
The only criterion is change in taste (طعم), color (لون), or smell (ريح). If none of these change, the water remains pure regardless of the impurity's presence.
The change must be perceptible by normal human senses.
3
Note the disregard of quantity
The passage explicitly says 'even if it is ten cubits'—meaning a large body of water (e.g., a deep well) is judged by the same standard. Quantity does not override the need for a property change.
This contradicts the notion that a large volume automatically purifies.
4
Identify the authorities
The ruling is upheld by al-Halwani and confirmed as the relied-upon position in al-Khulasa, Fatawa Qadikhan, and al-Muhit. These are authoritative later Hanafi works.
This gives the ruling weight as mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school considers water less than two qullas (approx. 270 L) impure upon contact with impurity even without change; water above that remains pure unless change occurs. The Hanafi school rejects the quantity threshold entirely.
This is the main inter-madhhab ikhtilaf on this issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحلواني : المعتبر الطعم أو اللون أو الريح فإن لم يتغير جاز وإلا فلا، ولو كان عشرة أذرع . قال في الخلاصة وفتاوى قاضيخان والتعويل عليه، وصححه في المحيط.
Al-Halwani: The considered factor is taste, color, or smell. If it does not change, it is permissible; otherwise not, even if it is ten cubits. It is said in al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadikhan: reliance is upon it, and it is corrected in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about water that has come into contact with an impurity. According to al-Halwani, the criterion for determining if the water is impure is whether its taste, color, or smell has changed. If none of these three properties are altered, the water remains pure and permissible for use. This applies regardless of the quantity of water, even if it is as much as ten cubits deep. The author notes that this view is relied upon in the later works al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadikhan, and it is considered correct (sahhaha) in al-Muhit. The passage type is a ruling statement with supporting authorities from the Hanafi school. The ruling is based on the principle that water is considered pure until a change in its properties indicates contamination.

Key Terms
المعتبر (al-mu'tabar): The considered factor or criterion for determining impurity
الطعم (al-ta'm): Taste – one of the three properties whose change indicates impurity.
اللون (al-lawn): Color – one of the three properties.
الريح (al-rayh): Smell – one of the three properties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for water being impure according to al-Halwani?
A) Quantity of water
B) Change in taste, color, or smell
C) Source of water
D) Depth of the well

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, water is judged pure unless its taste, color, or smell changes; quantity is irrelevant.

Mouse Dying in Non-Water Substances: Liquid vs Solid Ruling
Ruling on impurity spread based on substance state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for impurity when a mouse dies in a liquid versus a solid substance and apply the operational definition of liquid and solid from al-Mabsut.

Core Idea
Impurity spreads entirely in liquids but only locally in solids — the distinction hinges on immediate leveling when dented.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario: a mouse falls into a substance like oil or ghee. The ruling depends critically on whether the substance is liquid or solid. The author provides a precise operational definition from al-Mabsut and also discusses related issues such as selling such substances and using impure fat for tanning leather. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying purity laws in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two categories
The passage deals with a mouse dying in a substance other than water. Two categories are distinguished: liquid (mā'i') and solid (jāmid). Identify which category triggers which ruling.
2
State the ruling for each category
For liquid: the entire substance becomes impure, but it can be used for non-body purposes like lamps, though not in mosques. For solid: only the area around the mouse is removed; the rest stays pure.
3
Examine the definition of liquid vs solid
From al-Mabsut: if a dent levels immediately, it is liquid; if not, it is solid. Understand this operational test.
The Arabic word 'لوقوّر' likely means 'when one makes a dent in it'.
4
Apply the definition to a practical example
Consider ghee: if it is solid at room temperature, it is solid; if melted, it is liquid. The ruling changes accordingly.
5
Note additional rulings on tanning and sale
Leather tanned with such impure fat becomes pure after washing, and the buyer has the option to return if unaware of the impurity.
This highlights the practical scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ماتت الفأرة في غير الماء فإن كان مائعاً تنجس جميعه وجاز استعماله في غير الأبدان. كذا قالوا. وينبغي أن لا يستصبح به في المساجد لكونه ممنوعاً عن إدخال النجاسة المسجد، وتجوز بيعه وللمشتري الخيار إن لم يعلم به وإن كان جامداً ألقيت الفأرة وما حولها وكان الباقي طاهراً أو جاز الانتفاع بما حولها في غير الأبدان وفي المبسوط وحد الجمود والذوب أنه إذا كان بحال لوقوّر ذلك الموضع لا يستوي من ساعته فهو جامد، وإن كان يستوي من ساعته فهو ذائب . وذكر الاسبيجابي أن الجلد إذا دبغ بذلك السمن يغسل الجلد بالماء ويطهر والمتشرب فيه معفو عنه، ولمن اشتراه الخيار إن لم يعلم به.
And if a mouse dies in something other than water, then if it is liquid, all of it becomes impure and it is permissible to use it for other than bodies. Thus they said. And it is better not to make lamps from it in mosques because it is prohibited to bring impurity into the mosque. Its sale is permissible and the buyer has the option if he does not know of it. And if it is solid, the mouse and what is around it are thrown away, and the rest is pure, or it is permissible to benefit from what is around it for other than bodies. And in al-Mabsut, the definition of solid and liquid: if its condition is such that when that place is dented, it does not level immediately, it is solid; and if it levels immediately, it is liquid. And al-Isbijabi mentioned that if leather is tanned with that fat, the leather is washed with water and becomes pure, and what is absorbed is forgiven, and the buyer of it has the option if he does not know of it.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage contains rulings about a mouse falling into a substance other than water, such as oil or ghee. The author distinguishes between liquid (mā'i') and solid (jāmid) substances. If the substance is liquid, the entire substance becomes impure (najis), but it may still be used for non-consumption purposes such as lighting lamps, though not in mosques. It can be sold, but the buyer has the option to return it if unaware of the impurity. If the substance is solid, only the area around the mouse is removed, and the rest remains pure. The definition of solid vs. liquid is given from al-Mabsut: if you make a dent and it does not level immediately, it is solid; if it levels right away, it is liquid. Additionally, if leather is tanned with such impure fat, washing the leather renders it pure, and the absorbed portion is excused. The buyer again has the option. This passage type is a ruling statement with a proof based on defining terms.

Key Terms
مائع (mā'i'): Liquid substance that levels immediately when dented
جامد (jāmid): Solid substance that does not level immediately when dented

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire fat becomes impure
B) Only the mouse and surrounding area are removed
C) Wait for it to liquefy
D) Throw away all of it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between liquid and solid substances based on immediate leveling, and this determines the extent of impurity spread.

Mouse Dying in Wine That Becomes Vinegar
Ruling on vinegar made from wine in which a mouse died

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on vinegar from wine with a dead mouse and apply the preferred view based on decomposition and removal.

Core Idea
Vinegar from najis wine may be pure, but a dead mouse inside or decomposed changes the ruling.

This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj addresses a specific case: a mouse dies in wine, and then the wine becomes vinegar. The Hanafi school has multiple views on its permissibility, and the preferred opinion depends on two factors: whether the mouse decomposed and whether it was removed before or after the transformation. Understanding these conditions helps us apply the correct ruling in similar situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed issue
Read the passage and note that a mouse dies in wine, which later becomes vinegar. The question is whether the vinegar is permissible (halal) to drink or not, given that the wine was originally najis and the mouse is a dead animal (also najis).
The vinegar is a transformed substance; transformation (istihalah) often changes rulings.
2
Understand the three opinions
The passage mentions three opinions: some said the vinegar is permissible unconditionally; some said it is never permissible; and some said it depends on whether the mouse decomposed. Which one is preferred?
The third opinion is called 'ahsan' (best).
3
Recognize the preferred view and its conditions
The preferred view holds: if the mouse did not decompose (tafasakhat), the vinegar is permissible; if it decomposed, it is not permissible because parts of the mouse have mixed and cannot be removed. This applies only if the mouse was removed before the vinegar formed.
Decomposition means the body broke down and mixed with the liquid, spreading impurity.
4
Apply the additional condition about time of extraction
If the mouse remains in the wine while it becomes vinegar (i.e., not removed beforehand), then the vinegar is never permissible—even if not decomposed—because it is now in direct contact with a dead animal. The passage explicitly states this exception.
The ruling changes if the mouse is not removed before the transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج الوهاج : وإن ماتت الفأرة في الخمر فصار خلاً قال بعضهم : الخل مباح، وقيل لا يحل شربه، وقيل إذا لم تتفسخ فيه جاز وإن تفسخت لم يجز لأنه قد صار فيه جزء ومنها وهذا القول أحسن. وهذا إذا استخرجت منه قبل أن خلاً، أما إذا صار خلاً والفأرة فيه لا يحل شربه، سواء كانت متفسخة أو يصير لا لأنه نجس ا هـ .
And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If a mouse dies in wine and it becomes vinegar, some said: the vinegar is permissible. And it is said: its drinking is not lawful. And it is said: if it did not decompose in it, it is permissible; if it decomposed, it is not permissible because part of it has become from it, and this saying is best. This is if it was extracted before it became vinegar. But if it became vinegar while the mouse is in it, its drinking is not lawful, whether decomposed or not, because it is najis. End.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a mouse dies in wine, and then the wine becomes vinegar. There are three opinions: (1) The vinegar is permissible (mubah) because the wine is transformed into a pure substance. (2) It is not lawful to drink because the impurity remains. (3) The preferred view: if the mouse did not decompose, it is permissible; if it decomposed, it is not permissible because some of its parts have mixed. However, this applies only if the mouse was removed before the wine became vinegar. If the mouse remains in the wine while it becomes vinegar, it is never permissible because the vinegar is now najis due to direct contact with the dead mouse. This passage type displays an intra-Hanafi dispute and selects the best opinion.

Key Terms
تفسخت (tafasakhat): Decomposed, i.e., body broke down and mixed with the liquid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) Never
C) If mouse did not decompose and was removed before vinegar formation
D) Only if mouse was removed after vinegar formed

Key Takeaway
Vinegar from wine with a dead mouse is permissible only if the mouse did not decompose and was removed before transformation; otherwise it remains najis.

Well Dug as a Sewer: Purification Ruling
Ruling on a pit originally built as a sewer then used as a well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of water and sides when a sewer pit is converted into a well and distinguish the two excavation scenarios.

Core Idea
Water is pure if impurity depth is excavated; sides need wider digging to become pure.

In Hanafi fiqh, the impurity of a well originally used as a sewer is not permanent. This ruling from al-Muhit explains how the depth and width of excavation determine whether the water and the well's walls become pure. Understanding this condition-based distinction is essential for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and its legal status
The case is a pit (balūʿa) originally dug as a sewer that is later converted into a well. The ruling concerns the purity of the water and the sides of the well.
The act is converting a sewer to a well; the hukm is on the resulting purity.
2
Distinguish the two excavation scenarios
Scenario A: the well is dug exactly to the depth that the impurity had penetrated. Scenario B: the well is dug wider than the original impurity zone.
The depth of impurity penetration is presumed known from the sewer's use.
3
State the Hanafi ruling for each scenario
For Scenario A: the water is pure (tahir) but the sides remain impure (najis). For Scenario B: both the water and the entire well become pure.
4
Extract the underlying principle
Impurity is a physical entity that is removed by excavating the contaminated soil. The sides retain impurity if still in contact with original contamination; they become pure if the contaminated soil around them is removed as well.
This principle is used for analogous cases of contaminated earth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط والتنجيس : بالوعة حفروها وجعلوها بئر ماء، فإن حفروها مقدار ما وصلت إليه النجاسة فالماء طاهر وجوانبها نجسة وإن حفروها أوسع من الأول طهر الماء والبئر كله ا هـ .
And in al-Muhit: And al-Tanjis: A pit they dug and made it a well of water. If they dug it to the amount that the impurity reached, then the water is pure and its sides are impure. And if they dug it wider than the first, the water and the entire well become pure. End.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where people dug a pit initially as a sewer (balūʿa) and later converted it into a well for water. The ruling depends on the depth of the digging. If they dug only as deep as the impurity had penetrated, then the water that collects is pure (tahir) because the impurity has been removed by digging, but the sides of the well remain impure because they have been in contact with the impurity. However, if they dug the well wider (i.e., removed more soil than the original impurity reached), then both the water and the entire well (including sides) become pure because the impure soil has been entirely removed. This passage type is a ruling statement with a condition-based distinction.

Key Terms
بالوعة (bālūʿa): A sewer or cesspit dug for waste water

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Water and sides are pure
B) Water pure, sides impure
C) Water impure, sides pure
D) Both impure

Key Takeaway
The purity of a well converted from a sewer depends on whether the contaminated soil was completely removed; partial removal purifies water but not sides.

Ruling on Drawing Water When a Goose Dies in a Well
Ruling with analogy on well purification for a goose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for well purification when a goose dies, and apply the size-based analogy to determine the amount of water to draw.

Core Idea
The size of the animal determines the purification amount: small = partial, large = full.

This short passage from al-Khulasa provides a practical ruling for well purification. It teaches us that the amount of water to draw depends on the size of the animal that contaminated the well. A goose, depending on its size, is analogized to either a chicken (small) or a camel (large). This size-based classification is a distinctive feature of Hanafi fiqh, ensuring that the purification effort matches the level of contamination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its size
Determine if the goose is small (saghīr) or large (kabīr). Size is the key variable.
2
Apply the analogy
If small, treat like a chicken (limited drawing). If large, treat like a camel (full drawing). The analogy is based on the animal's volume affecting water impurity.
3
Execute the ruling
For small goose: draw a specified limited amount (e.g., 20 buckets in other texts). For large goose: draw all water (yanziḥu kull al-mā'). Full drawing is wajib for large animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : والأوز كالدجاج إن كان صغيراً، وإن كان كبيراً فهو كالجمل العظيم ينزح كل الماء.
And in al-Khulasa: Geese are like a chicken if small, and if large, it is like a huge camel, all water must be drawn.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This brief passage in al-Khulasa provides a rule for the amount of water to be drawn from a well if a goose falls in and dies. The size of the goose determines the purification method: if the goose is small, it is treated like a chicken, meaning only a limited amount of water needs to be drawn (typically as per the standard for poultry). If the goose is large, it is treated like a camel, requiring all the water in the well to be drawn out. This classification is based on the size and impact of the animal on the water. The passage type is a ruling statement with an analogy.

Key Terms

الأوز (al-awz): Goose

Purification of a Well by Flowing Water
Ruling that a well becomes pure by flow of water until some exits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that flowing water purifies a well and apply the qiyas to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A well is purified by outflow, not complete emptying—analogous to a basin.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir presents a clear ruling on the purification of a well by flowing water. The author uses qiyas (analogy) to a basin, establishing that the flow of water (jaryān al-mā') is the effective cause (illah) of purification. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish when outflow suffices versus when complete removal is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon and its legal outcome. The act is purifying a najis well by letting water flow out. The ruling is that the well becomes pure (tahir).
Focus on the verb 'tahurat' (became pure) and the condition 'khara ja ba‘duh' (some of it exits).
2
Locate the Proof (Qiyas)
Identify the evidence cited: the analogy to a basin (hawd). If a basin becomes impure, running water into it until some overflows purifies it. The well is analogous.
The text explicitly says 'wa sara ka al-hawd' (and it becomes like the basin).
3
Trace the Illah (Effective Cause)
Extract the common cause that connects the basin and the well. The cause is the flow of water (jaryān al-mā'), which removes impurity.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas because the cause is explicitly stated: 'li wujud sabab al-taharah wa huwa jaryān al-mā''.
4
Apply to Analogous Cases
Generalize the ruling to other water bodies where flow can occur, such as reservoirs, tanks, or swimming pools. The key condition is the existence of an outlet and sufficient flow.
Remember that the Hanafi school does not require complete emptying; outflow suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير : ولو تنجست بئر فأجرى ماؤها بأن حفر لها منفذ فصار الماء يخرج منه حتى خرج بعضه طهرت لوجود سبب الطهارة وهو جريان الماء وصار كالحوض إذا تنجس فأجرى فيه الماء حتى خرج بعضه وقد ذكرناه ا هـ .
And in Fath al-Qadir: If a well becomes impure and its water is made to flow by digging an outlet so that water comes out of it until some of it goes out, it becomes pure due to the existence of the cause of purity, which is the flow of water. And it becomes like a basin which, when impure, water is run into it until some goes out, and we have mentioned it. End.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage from Fath al-Qadir provides a ruling that if a well becomes impure, it can be purified by creating an outlet for the water to flow out. The flow of water is considered a cause (sabab) of purification. The analogy (qiyas) is made to a basin (hawd): if a basin becomes impure, running water into it until some overflows purifies it. Similarly, for a well, if water flows out until some has exited, the well becomes pure. This is based on the principle that flowing water carries away impurity. The passage type is a ruling with proof via analogy to a basin.

Key Terms
جريان الماء (jaryān al-mā'): Flow of water; considered a purifying agent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause (sabab) of purification for a well?
A) Complete emptying
B) Flow of water
C) Adding pure water
D) Removing all water

Key Takeaway
Flowing water purifies a well by outflow, without requiring complete emptying.

What Constitutes a Well: The Hanafi Definition
Refinement of the definition of a well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for what constitutes a well and apply it to examples of buried containers.

Core Idea
A well is any excavation beyond arm's reach, including buried jars, because the term derives from digging.

Wells have special purification rulings in taharah—for example, the water in a well is considered still (mā' muqaddar) and has specific rules about changing due to impurity. But what exactly counts as a well? This passage refines the definition to include large buried jars and reservoirs, settling earlier scholarly differences. The key insight is that the defining feature is depth beyond the reach of the hand, not whether the excavation is natural or man-made.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage begins by quoting al-Fawa'id: a buried jar (ḥubb maṭmūr) is treated like a well. Understand the problem: are man-made containers subject to the same rules as naturally dug wells?
This was a point of disagreement among earlier Hanafis.
2
Examine the transmitted position
Shaykh 'Alā' al-Dīn reports that the author of al-Tanwīr transmitted from al-Fawā'id that a jar buried most of it in the ground is like a well. This extends to reservoirs (ṣihrīj) and large vessels (zibār).
These are all cases where the vessel is too large or deep to reach the water by hand.
3
Understand the etymological argument
The word bi'r is derived from ba'arta meaning 'I dug'. Therefore, anything that is a result of digging (even if it is a man-made container placed in a hole) falls under the linguistic definition.
Etymology is used here as a rational (ta'līl) argument to justify inclusion.
4
Apply the refined criterion
Shaykh 'Alā' al-Dīn states the verified rule: every excavation in the ground that the hand cannot reach is in the ruling of a well. This is the muftā bihī criterion.
The hand's reach determines the limit: if you can touch the water, it is not a well.
5
Distinguish excluded cases
Items like a small jar (dann), a fountain (fusayniyya), and a natural spring ('ayn) are NOT wells. The reason given: well-related rulings are exceptional (khārija 'an al-qiyās), so other things cannot be attached to them by analogy.
This protects the special fiqh of wells from being expanded indiscriminately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشيخ علاء الدين في شرحه على التنوير: نقل المصنف يعني صاحب التنوير عن الفوائد أن الحب المطمور أكثره في الأرض كالبئر، وعليه فالصهريج والزبر الكبير منه كالبئر وقال : فاغتنم هذا التحرير اهـ والزبر الدن وهو الراقود العظيم وهو أطول من الحب لا يقعد إلا أن يحفر له كما في القاموس أقول وبالله التوفيق الذي ينبغي تحريره أن يقال : كل ما كان حفره في الأرض لا تناله اليد فهو في حكم البئر وداخل في مسماها لأنها كما مر مشتقة من بأرت أي حفرت فيكون الوارد فيها وارداً فيه بخلاف نحو الدن والفسقية والعين لأن مسائل الآبار خارجة عن القياس فلا يلحق بها غيرها، وبه يظهر ما نقله في النهر عن بعض أهل العصر وكذا ما نقلناه عن المقدسي، وإلى ما ذكرنا يشير صدر كلام النهر الذي قدمناه والله تعالى أعلم.
And Shaykh Ala al-Din said in his commentary on al-Tanwir: The author, meaning the author of al-Tanwir, transmitted from al-Fawa'id that a jar (hubb) buried most of it in the ground is like a well. Based on this, a reservoir (sihrij) and a large zibār among them are like a well. He said: So seize this verification. And al-zibār is a large jar (dann), which is a huge raqūd, and it is longer than a jar; it cannot stand unless dug for it, as in al-Qamus. I say: With Allah's success, what should be verified is to say: Everything that is dug in the ground and cannot be reached by hand is in the ruling of a well and falls under its name because, as mentioned, it is derived from 'ba'arta' meaning 'I dug', so what enters it enters the same ruling. Unlike a jar (dann), a fountain (fusayniyya), or a spring (ayn), because the issues of wells are outside analogy, so other things are not attached to them. And through this, what is transmitted in al-Nahr from some contemporaries becomes clear, as well as what we transmitted from al-Maqdisi. And the beginning of the statement of al-Nahr that we presented points to what we mentioned. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Shaykh Ala al-Din in his commentary on al-Tanwir. He discusses the definition of a well (bi'r) for the purposes of purification rulings. The author of al-Tanwir reported from al-Fawa'id that a buried jar (hubb matmur) – i.e., a large earthenware jar with most of it sunk into the ground – is treated like a well. This leads to the conclusion that a reservoir (sihrij) and a large zibār (a huge vessel) are also like a well. Shaykh Ala al-Din then presents his own verification: anything dug into the ground that cannot be reached by hand (i.e., too deep or large to handle) is considered a well and falls under the same rulings. This is because the word 'bi'r' is derived from 'ba'arta' meaning 'I dug'. Therefore, any such excavation takes the legal status of a well, and rulings specific to wells (like purification by drawing water) apply to them. However, other containers like a small jar (dann), a fountain (fusayniyya), or a natural spring (ayn) are not considered wells because well-related rules are exceptional and cannot be extended through analogy (qiyas). The passage thus refines the understanding by providing a clear criterion: depth beyond arm's reach. The passage type is a definitional ruling with reasoning (ta'lil).

Key Terms
بئر (bi'r): Any excavation in the ground too deep to be reached by hand
مشتقة من بأرت (mushtaqqa min ba'arta): Derived from 'I dug', thus any dug object
الحب المطمور (al-hubb al-matmur): A large earthen jar buried most of it into the ground
زبر (zibār): A huge jar (dann) that cannot stand without digging

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shaykh 'Alā' al-Dīn's verification, what is the definitive criterion for an excavation to be considered a well?
A) It must be at least 10 cubits deep
B) It must be lined with stones
C) The hand cannot reach the water inside
D) It must be naturally occurring, not man-made

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines a well as any excavation beyond hand's reach, including buried jars and reservoirs, because the ruling is tied to the act of digging.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Well Purification Buckets
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bucket count for well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the number of buckets to draw from a replenishing well and apply one of the methods to determine the required amount.

Core Idea
Well purification: no single number; the Hanafi school offers multiple valid estimations.

When a well becomes impure and its water replenishes as you draw, scholars differed on how many buckets must be drawn. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Nasr, along with the criteria for fatwa and the preferred operative opinion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Identify the scenario: a well that continuously replenishes water, making complete emptying impossible. The base ruling (nazh kull) must be adapted.
2
List the opinions
Abu Hanifa: 100 buckets or until overwhelmed; Muhammad: 200 or 300 (based on Baghdad wells); Abu Yusuf: two measurement methods (test pit or reed); Abu Nasr: two expert estimators.
3
Identify the bases
Each opinion is rooted in observation of local wells (Kufa vs Baghdad), methodological preference (practical vs precise), and legal principles (obedience according to ability).
4
Examine the tarjih
al-Khulasa prefers 300; al-Ikhtiyar notes ease of Muhammad's view but weakness; Husam al-Din delegates to the afflicted; Abu Nasr's expert method called 'closest to fiqh'.
5
Apply the reasoning
Given a new well in a different region, choose among the methods based on context and precaution. Practice using the expert estimation method as the recommended approach.
This step prepares for the practice tasks.
