⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومائتان) لو لم يكن نزحها أي ينزح مائتا دلو إن كانت البئر معينة لا يمكن نزحها بسبب أنهما كلما نزحوا نبع من أسفله مثل ما نزحوا أو أكثر. وقد اختلفت الروايات فيها فما في الكتاب مروي عن محمد قالوا إنما أفتى به بناء على ما شاهد في بغداد لأن الغالب ماء آبارها كان لا يزيد على ثلثمائة وروي عن أبي حنيفة التقدير بمائة دلو قالوا : أفتى بذلك بناء على قلة المياه في آبار الكوفة. وفي الهداية وعن أبي حنيفة في الجامع الماء الصغير في مثله ينزح حتى يغلبهم ولم يقدر الغلبة بشيء كما دأبه هو في مثله ا هـ . وإنما لم يقدرها لأنها متفاوتة والنزح إلى أن يظهر العجز أمر صحيح في الشرع لأن الطاعة الطاقة. وقيل: على قول أبي حنيفة يجب قدر ما يغلب على ظنهم أنه جميع الماء عند ابتداء النزح والأصح تفسير الغلبة بالعجز . كذا ذكر قاضيخان. وعن أبي يوسف وجهان: أحدهما أن تحفر حفيرة عمقها ودورها مثل موضع الماء منها وتخصص على قول بعض المشايخ ويصب فيها، فإذا امتلأت فقد نزح ماؤها. والثاني أن ترسل قصبة الماء ويجعل علامة لمبلغ في ثم ينزح عشر دلاء مثلاً، ثم تعاد القصبة فينظركم انتقص ؛ فإن انتقص العشر فهو مائة قالوا : ولكن هذا لا يستقيم إلا إذا كان دور البئر من أول حد الماء إلى قعر البئر متساوياً، وإلا لا يلزم إذا نقص شبر بنزح عشر من أعلى الماء أن ينقص شبر بنزح مثله من أسفله. وعن أبي نصر محمد بن سلام أنه يؤتى برجلين لهما بصارة بأمر الماء ؛ فإذا قدراه بشيء وجب نزح ذلك القدر وهو الأصح والأشبه بالفقه وفي معراج الدراية أنه المختار لكونهما نصاب الشهادة الملزمة، واشتراط المعرفة لهما بالماء باعتبار أن الأحكام إنما تستفاد ممن له علم، أصله قول تعالى فاسئلوا أهل الذكر إن كنت لا تعلمون. وظاهر ما في النقاية الاكتفاء بواحد لأنه أمر ديني فيكتفي بالواحد، لكن أكثر الكتب على الاثنين وقد القول جماعة واختاروه وصحح الإمام حسام الدين في شرح الجامع الصغير اعتبار الغلبة العجز، وذكر أن الفتوى على أنه يفوض إلى رأي المبتلى به. وفي الخلاصة أن الفتوى على أنه ينزح ثلثمائة، وكذا في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى فتاوى العتابي أن المختار ما عن محمد. فالحاصل أنه قد اختلف التصحيح في المسألة واختلفت الفتوى فيها، والإفتاء بما عن محمد أسهل على الناس والعمل بما عن أبي نصر أحوط ، ولهذا قال في الاختيار: وما روي عن محمد أيسر على الناس لكن لا يخفى ضعفه فإنه إذا كان الحكم الشرعي نزح جميع للحكم بنجاسته، فالقول بطهارة البئر بالاقتصار على نزح عدد مخصوص من الدلاء يتوقف
His saying: (And two hundred) if its drawing is not possible, i.e., draw two hundred buckets if the well is a specific one whose water cannot be completely drawn because every time they draw, water gushes from its bottom equal to what they drew or more. And the reports differ on it. What is in the book is narrated from Muhammad; they said: he only gave the fatwa based on what he observed in Baghdad because usually the water of its wells did not exceed three hundred. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa to estimate one hundred buckets; they said he gave fatwa based on the scarcity of water in the wells of Kufa. And in al-Hidaya: and from Abu Hanifa in al-Jami', for a small well, drawing continues until they are overwhelmed, and he did not estimate overwhelm (ghalaba) with any number, as is his custom in similar issues. He did not estimate it because it varies, and drawing until inability appears is sound in Sharia because obedience is according to ability. And it is said: according to Abu Hanifa, the amount that they think is all the water at the beginning of drawing is obligatory, and the correct interpretation of ghalaba is inability. Thus mentioned Qadikhan. And from Abu Yusuf there are two ways: first, dig a pit of the same depth and circumference as the water place from it, and assign on the saying of some mashayikh, and pour into it; when it is filled, its water has been drawn. Second, send a reed of water and make a mark at the level, then draw ten buckets for example, then return the reed and see how much it decreased; if it decreased by ten, then it is one hundred. They said: but this is only valid if the circumference of the well from the first water level to the bottom is equal, otherwise it does not follow that a handspan decrease from drawing ten from top would equal a handspan decrease from drawing similar from bottom. And from Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam: two men who are experts in water matters are brought; when they estimate something, that amount must be drawn. This is the most correct and closest to fiqh. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya it is the chosen view because they constitute a complete evidence-holding pair (nisab al-shahada), and requiring them to have knowledge of water is based on the fact that rulings are obtained from those who have knowledge; its origin is His saying: 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know.' The apparent in al-Niqaya is sufficiency of one because it is a religious matter, so one is sufficient. But most books say two, and a group chose it. And Imam Husam al-Din in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir considered the correct view to be the ghalaba of inability, and he mentioned that the fatwa is delegated to the opinion of the afflicted one. And in al-Khulasa, the fatwa is that three hundred are drawn, and similarly in Mi'raj al-Diraya citing from Fatawa al-'Atabi, the chosen is what is from Muhammad. So in summary, there is difference in verification and fatwa. Giving fatwa with Muhammad's view is easier for the people, while acting on Abu Nasr's view is more precautionary. That is why it is said in al-Ikhtiyar: What is narrated from Muhammad is easier for people, but its weakness is not hidden, for if the legal ruling is to draw all due to the judgment of impurity, then the saying of the well's purity by limiting to a specific number of buckets depends...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This is a lengthy intra-Hanafi dispute about the number of buckets to draw from a well that has become impure. The base ruling is that if a well becomes impure, all water should be drawn. However, in wells where water replenishes (e.g., springs), it is impossible to empty it. Hence, various estimates are given. The text mentions multiple opinions: (1) According to Imam Muhammad, 200 buckets; (2) According to Abu Hanifa, 100 buckets (based on Kufa's shallow wells); (3) Abu Hanifa also had a view of drawing until 'overwhelmed' (ghalaba), interpreted as inability; (4) Abu Yusuf had two methods – digging a test pit or using a reed to measure; (5) Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam prefers appointing two expert watermen. The text then discusses which is the correct (sahih) view and which is given as fatwa. It notes that Imam Muhammad's view (200 or 300?) is easier but acting on Abu Nasr's is more precautionary. Imam Husam al-Din considered the view of delegating to the opinion of the afflicted one. In al-Khulasa, the fatwa is 300. The author ultimately notes the variation in verification and fatwa. This passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with multiple positions and a discussion of the mufta bihi. The argument type is partly rational (qiyas khafi) as some methods involve analogy and reasoning.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalaba): The state of being overwhelmed or unable to continue drawing; marks the point of purification
نصاب الشهادة (nisāb al-shahāda): The minimum number of witnesses required for legal testimony, i.e., two men

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always 100 buckets
B) Always 300 buckets
C) By bringing two expert watermen
D) By measuring the well's depth

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers multiple valid methods for determining well purification buckets, with the choice between easier and precautionary approaches depending on context.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Nazh: How Much Water to Draw?
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute regarding nazh quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the multiple Hanafi positions on nazh from a contaminated well and explain the basis and tarjih (preference) of each position, particularly the mufta bihi view of expert estimation.

Core Idea
No single fixed number for nazh: the ruling varies by well conditions, and expert estimation is the soundest Hanafi position.

When an animal falls into a well and contaminates the water, the well must be purified by drawing water (nazh). But how much water must be drawn? The Hanafi school has multiple opinions on this, reflecting different circumstances and principles. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, exploring the views of the founding imams and the tarjih process that leads to the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the legal problem? A well cannot be fully emptied because water continuously springs. The question is: how many buckets must be drawn to ensure the well is pure?
The ruling is wajib, but the quantity is disputed.
2
List the positions of the Hanafi imams
State the three main positions: Muhammad (200 buckets), Abu Hanifa (100 buckets or draw until exhaustion), Abu Yusuf (two measurement methods), and Abu Nasr (expert estimation).
Each position is based on different observations and principles.
3
Analyze the basis of each position
Examine why each imam chose his opinion: Muhammad based on Baghdad wells, Abu Hanifa on Kufa's scarcity, Abu Yusuf on practical measurement, Abu Nasr on expert testimony.
The verse 'Ask the people of remembrance' supports expert estimation.
4
Compare and contrast the positions
Note the differences: fixed numbers vs. flexible methods, ease vs. precaution, reliance on observation vs. expertise.
The commentaries highlight that Muhammad's view is easier, Abu Nasr's is more precautionary.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and reasoning
Identify that later scholars (like al-Marghinani, al-Hidayah) consider expert estimation as the soundest, though fatwa may vary by region. Understand the tarjih mechanism: testimony of experts is a binding proof.
The fatwa differs: some give fatwa by Muhammad's 200, others by Abu Nasr's estimation.

His saying: (and two hundred) if the well cannot be fully drawn, meaning two hundred buckets are drawn if the well is such that it cannot be drawn completely because whenever they draw, water springs from its bottom like what they drew or more. Narrations have differed on this: what is in the book is narrated from Muhammad—they said he gave a fatwa based on what he observed in Baghdad because the water of its wells mostly did not exceed three hundred. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of nazh (drawing water from a well) when an animal falls in and the well cannot be fully emptied. The commentator explains the different narrations from the founding imams. According to Muhammad (in the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq), the requirement is two hundred buckets if the well is such that water continuously springs. That was based on his observation in Baghdad. Abu Hanifa had two positions: one narration says one hundred buckets (based on Kufa wells), another says to draw until the drawer is exhausted (ghalaba) without a fixed number. Abu Yusuf has two methods: digging an equivalent pit or using a stick to measure the decrease. Abu Nasr advocates appointing two water experts to estimate the total water, which is the soundest view. The text discusses the preferability of these views. Ultimately, the fatwa varies: Muhammad's view is easier but weak, Abu Nasr's is more precautionary. The preferred view among later scholars is to refer to the opinion of the person affected (al-mubtala bihi) or to appoint experts. This passage shows the richness of Hanafi fiqh in accommodating different circumstances.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Drawing water from a well to remove impurity.
غلبة (ghalaba): Exhaustion or inability to continue drawing.
بصارة (basara): Expertise or experience in assessing water quantities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abu Nasr's view of expert estimation considered the soundest (al-asahh)?
A) It is based on a direct hadith from the Prophet (PBUH).
B) It relies on the Quranic verse 'Ask the people of remembrance' and the principle that two witnesses provide binding testimony.
C) It was the opinion of all three founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
D) It is the easiest ruling for the people.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers multiple valid positions on nazh; expert estimation is the most rigorous, but easier opinions may be given fatwa when needed.

Well Contamination Period: Bloated vs Non-Bloated Animals
Ruling on well contamination period with linguistic and juristic clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the contamination period of a well when a dead animal is found, distinguish between bloated and non-bloated cases, and apply the principle that doubt does not invalidate certainty.

Core Idea
A bloated animal pollutes the well for three days prior; a non-bloated one for one day; certainty is not overridden by doubt.

This lesson examines a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on how long a well remains contaminated after a dead animal is discovered in it. The passage distinguishes between bloated and non-bloated animals and clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'three days'. It also addresses the important fiqh principle that if pure water was used from a well later found to be contaminated, the purity of the person who used it remains intact due to certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
The ruling applies when a dead animal is found in a well and the time of its fall is unknown. Determine the contamination period: if the animal is bloated, it is three days; if not, one day and one night.
The period is counted backward from the time of discovery.
2
Understand the linguistic clarification
The commentator explains that 'three days' means three nights and the days that accompany them, using the Quranic verse 'four months and ten' (2:234) as an analogy. This resolves a potential misinterpretation by al-Zayla'i.
In Arabic, 'yam' can refer to daytime, but context may include the night.
3
Apply the principle of certainty
If someone performed wudu or washed clothes using this water while already in a state of purity, they do not repeat their prayers or washing, because purity is established with certainty and doubt does not remove it.
This is by consensus (ijma).
4
Recognize the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position is exactly as stated: 3 days for bloated, 1 day for non-bloated, with the linguistic clarification accepted.
No intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (ونجسها منذ ثلاث فأرة منتفخة جهل وقت وقوعها وإلا مذ يوم وليلة) أي نجس البئر منذ ثلاثة أيام بلياليها فأرة ميته منتفخة لا يدري وقت وقوعها وإن لم تكن منتفخة نجسها يوم وليلة . .. قال المصنف في المستصفى : أي مذ ثلاث ليال إذ لو أريد به الأيام لقال مذ ثلاثة لكن الليالي تنتظم ما بإزائها من الأيام كما أن الأيام تنتطم ما بازائها من الليالي كقوله تعالى أربعة أشهر وعشراً [البقرة : ٢٣٤] أي وعشر ليال بأيامها .اهـ. فعلم أنه لا حاجة إلى ما ذكر الزيلعي هنا أعلم أن البئر تنجس من وقت وقوع الحيوان الذي وجد ميتاً فيها إن ذلك الوقت وإن لم يعلم فقد صار الماء مشكوكاً في طهاته ونجاسته، فإذا توضؤوا منها وهم متوضؤون أو غسلوا ثيابهم من غير نجاسة فإنهم لا يعيدون إجماعاً لأن الطهارة لا تبطل
His saying: (and it becomes contaminated from three [days] for a bloated mouse when the time of its fall is unknown, otherwise from a day and a night). Meaning the well is contaminated for three days including their nights for a dead bloated mouse when the time of its fall is unknown; if it is not bloated, it is contaminated for a day and a night. The author said in al-Mustasfa: i.e., for three nights, because if he meant days he would have said 'three', but nights include the corresponding days, just as days include the corresponding nights, like His saying 'four months and ten' (2:234), i.e., ten nights with their days. So it is known that there is no need for what al-Zayla'i mentioned here. Know that the well becomes contaminated from the time the animal that is found dead fell into it; if that time is unknown, the water becomes doubtful in its purity and impurity. So if they perform ablution from it while already in a state of wudu, or wash their clothes without impurity, they do not repeat [the prayer] by consensus, because purity is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on how long a well is considered contaminated when a dead animal, specifically a mouse, is found in it. The text distinguishes between a bloated mouse and one that is not bloated. If the animal is bloated, the well is considered contaminated for three days prior to the discovery (counting backwards from the time of finding, assuming the fall occurred at an unknown time). If not bloated, the contamination period is one day and one night. The commentator clarifies that 'three days' means three nights and the days that accompany them, using the Quranic analogy of 'four months and ten'. This is a response to al-Zayla'i's comment. Additionally, the text notes that if water is used from a well that later turns out to be contaminated, and the user was already in a state of purity, their purity is not invalidated by consensus because the water was merely doubtful. This shows the principle that certainty is not overridden by doubt.

Key Terms
فأرة منتفخة (fa'ra muntafakha): A dead mouse that is bloated due to decomposition.
يوم وليلة (yawm wa layla): A full day and night (24 hours).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three days and three nights
B) One day and one night
C) Only from the moment of discovery
D) The well is not contaminated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets a practical backward-counting period for well contamination based on the bloated condition of the dead animal, and protects past acts performed with certainty.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Retroactive Well Impurity (Dead Mouse)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on retroactive well impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions (Imam Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn, mediated) on retroactive well impurity and apply the mediated view to practical scenarios involving acts of worship, clothes, and dough.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school balances ihtiyat and raf' al-haraj: Imam's caution for worship, Sahibayn's leniency for other matters.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling of a well when a dead mouse is found. The basic rule sets retroactive periods based on whether the mouse is bloated or not. However, the core issue is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: does the impurity affect past acts performed with the water? Imam Abu Hanifa adopts a precautionary approach, requiring repetition of prayers and washing of clothes. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) favor removing hardship, holding no retroactive effect. Later scholars, notably Nuh Afendi, mediate between these extremes, distinguishing acts of worship from other matters. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual evidence, ihtiyat, and the principle of raf' al-haraj.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basic Ruling for Retroactive Period
Identify the two scenarios: if a bloated mouse is found and time unknown, the well is impure from three days back; if not bloated, from one day and night. This sets the temporal scope for the dispute.
Bloating indicates longer contact; non-bloating indicates recent fall.
2
Locate the Core Dispute
The dispute is whether the impurity is attributed retroactively to affect previous acts (wudu, ghusl, washing clothes, dough). The text contrasts the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
The dispute hinges on the balance between ihtiyat and raf' al-haraj.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position
Imam holds that the well is impure from three days back for all purposes. Therefore, prayers performed with that water must be repeated, clothes washed, and dough not eaten.
This is based on precaution (ihtiyat) to avoid any doubt in worship.
4
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that there is no retroactive impurity. The well is considered pure for all past acts; nothing needs to be repeated or washed.
This is based on removal of hardship (raf' al-haraj) and the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
5
Analyze the Mediated Position
Later scholars like Nuh Afendi mediate: apply Imam's view to acts of worship (wudu, ghusl) as precaution, and Sahibayn's view to other matters (clothes, dough) to remove hardship. However, clothes must still be washed as a precaution against suspected impurity, but prayers in them are not repeated. Dough is permissible to eat.
This balanced view is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يمكن نزحها ونجسها منذ ثلاث فأرة منتفخة جهل وقت وقوعها وإلا مذ يوم وليلة

فما الفرق بينها وبين الطهارة عن حدث؟ وأيضاً إذا كان لزوم غسلها لكونها مغسولة بماء البئر لا للنجاسة التي كانت بها كما هو ظاهر كلام شارح المنية فما الفرق بين هذه الثياب وبين ما إذا غسلت لا عن نجاسة فإن اعتبارين الأول الاحتياط والتنزه ومقتضاه الحكم بنجاستها منذ ثلاثة أيام في حق الوضوء وغيره فتعاد الصلاة وتغسل الثياب ولا يؤكل العجين وهو اختيار الإمام. والثاني نفي الحرج ومقتضاه عدم الحكم بالنجاسة مطلقاً فلا تجب شيء مما مر وهو اختيارهما. والأول في نهاية الحرج والثاني في نهاية التوسعة فتوسط بينهما بأن خص رأي الإمام بالتوضؤ والاغتسال احتياطاً بالعبادة ورأيهما بما عداه لنفي الحرج ولكن أمعن النظر في الثياب فقال : يجب غسلها حذراً عن النجاسة المتوهمة وإن لم يجزم بسبقها ولم يجزم بإعادة ما صلاه بتلك الثياب نفياً للحرج، ولا بأس بأكل العجين .اهـ.
The Book of Purity. It is possible to drain it, and its impurity is from three (days) for a bloated mouse if the time of its fall is unknown, otherwise from a day and night. And if difficult... on my hearing benefits it. Where is that? Rather the reported from Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr is contrary to it. And some later scholars chose that the most apparent (view) is: if it is possible to block the sources of water without difficulty, they are blocked and the water in it is removed. That, if it is known that the location of the water in it is uniform in length and width in all its parts, a reed is sent into the water and one acts according to what we have presented. If that is not known, then if it is possible to act by its measure from two just men who have insight into wells, their statement is taken. If knowledge of the amount of water from two just men who are knowledgeable about that is difficult, they drain until they show inability according to their predominant opinion. Good for the contemplative, so let action be upon it. And this is a detail. His saying: 'And its impurity is from three (days) for a bloated mouse if the time of its fall is unknown, otherwise from a day and night' means: the well is impure from three days with their nights for a dead bloated mouse whose time of fall is unknown. If it is not bloated, it is impure from a day and night. The author said in al-Mustasfa: meaning from three nights. If he meant days, he would have said 'three' (masculine). But nights include their corresponding days, just as days include their corresponding nights, like His saying: 'four months and ten' meaning ten nights with their days. So it is known that there is no need for what al-Zayla'i mentioned here. Know that the well becomes impure from the time of the fall of the animal found dead in it, if that time is unknown, then the water has become doubtful in its purity and impurity. If they perform wudu from it while they are already in wudu, or wash their clothes from non-najasa, they do not repeat unanimously because purity is not invalidated by doubt. If they perform wudu from it while they are in hadath, or perform ghusl from janaba, or wash their clothes from najasa, then concerning the third (clothes) they do not repeat, but rather it is necessary for them to wash them according to the sound opinion. And it is judged to be impure at present without attribution because it is from the chapter of finding najasa in the garment. And whoever finds najasa in his garment more than the size of a dirham and does not know when it affected him, he does not repeat any of his prayers by agreement, and that is the sound (view). Thus in al-Muhit and al-Tabyin. And the commentator of Munyat al-Musalli criticized this by saying: if it is necessary for them to wash them because they were washed with the well water previously, at the time of knowing that the well contains the mouse without a day and night or without three days, how can it be? 'The analogy is that he would say six' because 'fara'id' is plural of 'farida'. His saying: 'because they were washed with the well water previously.' I say: 'ma' means 'time' and the adverb is related to 'washed', and his saying 'hal' is the object of 'taqaddama' like 'and fear a day' which is not an adverb. And 'ishtimal' is related to 'ilm', and 'bidun' is related to 'taqaddum'. The meaning: if it is necessary for them to wash them because they were washed with the well water at a previous time without a day and night or without three days from the time of knowledge of the mouse, how would the garments not be considered impugnated with certainty of the previous washing? The restriction of its being previous to within a day and night or three days is because if it were more than that from the time of its existence, nothing would be necessary because there is no ruling of its occurrence then. Also it is problematic: the najasa that was on the garment is certain, and its removal by this water is doubtful, so what is the difference between it and purity from hadath? Also, if the necessity of washing them is because they were washed with the well water, not because of the najasa that was on them, as is apparent from the commentator's words, what is the difference between these garments and if they were washed not for najasa? For the apparent meaning of his words is that it is not obligatory to wash them, but the apparent meaning of al-Zayla'i's words is that it is obligatory to wash them absolutely, for he said: His saying 'its impurity is from three' means concerning wudu, such that they must repeat the prayer if they performed wudu from it. As for other than that, it is judged impure without attribution because it is from the chapter of finding najasa in the garment, such that if they washed the garments with its water, only washing them is necessary according to the sound (view). Similar is in al-Durar, al-Minah, Sharh al-Multaqa by al-Bahansi, and similar in Mir'aj al-Diraya. Likewise al-Quduri said in his Mukhtasar: They repeated (prayers) if they performed wudu from it and washed everything that its water touched. And in al-Munya, al-Quduri's exact wording is mentioned, but the objection of the commentator of al-Munya against al-Zayla'i and his followers remains: if the impurity is judged at present, how can washing these garments be obligatory? Therefore some virtuous persons in the glosses of Sadr al-Shari'ah said: In al-Zayla'i's words there is confusion, such that some omitted the exception from his words. But the scholar Nuh Afandi, commentator of al-Durar wa al-Ghurur, explained it by saying: In the mentioned well there are two considerations: first, precaution and purity, which requires judging its impurity from three days concerning wudu and other things, so prayers are repeated, garments are washed, and dough is not eaten, and this is the choice of Imam (Abu Hanifa). Second, removal of hardship, which requires not judging impurity absolutely, so nothing of what was mentioned is obligatory, and this is their choice (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The first is in the extreme of hardship, the second in the extreme of leniency. So he mediated between them by restricting Imam's view to wudu and ghusl as a precaution for worship, and their view to other than that to remove hardship. But he considered the garments carefully and said: washing them is obligatory as a precaution against the suspected impurity, even if he is not certain of its prior existence, and he is not certain about repeating prayers performed in those garments to remove hardship, and there is no harm in eating dough.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the ruling of a well when a dead mouse is found. It first states the basic rule: if a bloated mouse is found and the time of its fall is unknown, the water is considered impure retroactively three days back. If not bloated, one day and night. The author then clarifies the wording 'three' meaning nights, citing the Quranic verse to show that nights include their corresponding days. He dismisses al-Zayla'i's alternative interpretation. The core of the passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the retroactive effect: does the impurity affect previous acts of worship and washing? If people performed wudu or ghusl or washed clothes during that time, must they repeat? The commentators discuss various positions. Al-Zayla'i holds that for wudu and ghusl, prayers must be repeated, but for clothes, only washing is necessary, and no repetition of prayers in them because the najasa is considered present now without attribution to a specific time. The commentator of Munyat al-Musalli objects that if clothes were washed with the well water, they become impure and must be washed again, creating a circularity. Nuh Afendi resolves this by noting two considerations: the precautionary view of Imam Abu Hanifa (retroactive for all) and the lenient view of the two companions (no retroactivity). The mediated view is that for acts of worship (wudu/ghusl) the stricter view applies, but for other things (clothes, dough) the lenient view applies, though clothes must be washed as a precaution. This shows the Hanafi method of balancing precaution (ihtiyat) and removal of hardship (raf' al-haraj).

Key Terms
فأرة منتفخة (fa'ratun muntafikhah): A dead mouse that is bloated/swollen, indicating it has been in the water for some time.
نزح (nazh): Draining or removing water from the well to purify it.
نجس (najis): Impure; the water is considered legally impure and cannot be used for purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mediated position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on dough made with water from a well later found to contain a dead mouse?
A) It must be thrown away because it is impure.
B) It is permissible to eat.
C) It is impure but can be eaten if there is no alternative.
D) It is makruh to eat.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances ihtiyat and raf' al-haraj: for well impurity from a dead mouse, the mediated position applies precaution to acts of worship and leniency to other matters, with an additional precaution for washing clothes.

Well Water with Dead Mouse: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well water using istihsan vs qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on well water with a dead mouse, explain their respective proofs (istihsan and qiyas), and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa distinguishes ibadat from garments; Sahibayn apply 'certainty not removed by doubt' uniformly.

When a dead mouse is found in a well, does the water become retrospectively najis? The Hanafi school is divided. Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to differentiate between acts of worship and clothes, while the Sahibayn apply strict qiyas based on the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt'. This lesson unpacks their arguments and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A well is used for wudu, ghusl, and washing clothes. Later, a dead mouse is found. Determine the legal status of actions performed before discovery.
Assume the mouse has been in the well for less than three days (the period within which a dead mouse typically pollutes water according to Hanafi fiqh).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For wudu/ghusl (ibadat): prayers performed must be repeated. For washed clothes: only rewash the clothes, no repetition of prayers. This is his istihsan-based ruling.
The distinction is based on the hidden nature of the well vs visible najasah on clothes.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
No repetition or rewashing required for anything done before discovery. Their proof: qiyas on finding najasah on a garment without knowing when it occurred, and the maxim 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk'.
They consider the water pure until the moment of discovery; the mouse might have fallen after use.
4
Analyze thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if one performed wudu/ghusl within three days, all prayers in that state must be repeated. Under Sahibayn, no action is required.
This difference directly affects the worshipper's obligation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Zayla'i, Ibn Nujaym) adopt Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi, balancing caution (for ibadat) and ease (for clothes).
The tarjih is based on the strength of istihsan in this context.

…with doubt, and if they performed wudu from it while in a state of hadath, or performed ghusl from janabah, or washed their clothes from najasah, then regarding the third (clothes) they do not repeat (prayers), but only washing them is incumbent upon them according to the correct view. And it is ruled that the clothes are najis at present without ascription (to the past) because it falls under the case of finding najasah on the garment. And whoever finds on his garment najasah more than a dirham's amount and does not know when it afflicted him, does not repeat any of his prayers by consensus, and that is the correct view. Thus in al-Muhit and al-Tabyin. The commentator of Munyat al-Musalli objected: if it is incumbent upon them to wash them because they were washed with water from the well in a past time, at the time of knowledge that the well contained the mouse, within a day and night or within three days, how can it be that the najasah of the clothes is based on (the past)?…
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a dead mouse is found in a well. The text begins by stating the correct ruling according to the author: if people used the well water for wudu or ghusl, they must repeat their prayers; if they washed clothes with it, they need only rewash the clothes but do not repeat prayers prayed in them. This is based on the principle that najasah on a garment is treated differently from hadath. The commentator then raises an objection: if the clothes are considered najis only from the moment of discovery, why must they be washed? The discussion elaborates on the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa makes a distinction: for acts of worship (wudu, ghusl) the ruling is stricter (repeat), while for clothes the ruling is lenient (only wash, no redo of prayers). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the water is considered pure before discovery; hence no repetition or washing is required for anything done before discovery. They base this on qiyas: certainty is not removed by doubt, and the mouse might have fallen in after usage. Abu Hanifa uses istihsan: when the cause is hidden, it is necessary to attribute the effect to the apparent cause. The passage also addresses objections about the consistency of the rulings and attempts to reconcile different authorities such as al-Zayla'i and the commentator of Munyat al-Musalli.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons strict analogy in favor of a more suitable ruling due to necessity or public interest.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; comparing a new case to an established one in search of a common effective cause.
اليقين لا يزول بالشك (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk): A legal maxim: certainty is not removed by doubt; used by Sahibayn to argue past purity remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling under Abu Hanifa if someone washed their clothes with water from a well later found to have a dead mouse, and then prayed in those clothes?
A) They must repeat the prayers and wash the clothes.
B) They need not repeat the prayers but must wash the clothes.
C) They must repeat the prayers but need not wash the clothes.
D) They need do nothing.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position on well water with a dead mouse distinguishes between acts of worship (wudu/ghusl require prayer repetition) and garments (only rewash, no prayer repetition).

Ruling on Well Water with Dead Mouse: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repeating past salah after finding dead mouse in well water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding repetition of past salah when a dead mouse is found in well water, and state the proofs of each position.

Core Idea
Certainty of past purity is not removed by doubt, but the apparent cause of contamination demands precaution – a tension between qiyas and istihsan.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a dead mouse is found in a well, does one need to repeat all the prayers performed using that water? Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ, each with compelling proofs rooted in usul al-fiqh. The discussion hinges on the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' and the method of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds: if the mouse is swollen, repeat prayers of three days; if not, one day.
The swelling indicates the mouse died earlier, so the water was likely impure for longer.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that no repetition is required; the water is ruled impure only from the time of discovery.
Their view is based on the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze their qiyas: they analogize to impurity found on a garment, a woman's doubt about blood, and inheritance cases. The common illa is that events are attributed to the nearest possible time.
They also cite Abu Yusuf's anecdote of a kite dropping a mouse.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan: when the actual cause is hidden, the apparent cause (the water contamination) must be relied upon, similar to murder cases.
He distinguishes the well from a visible garment: the well is hidden, so we cannot rely on the default purity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the person must repeat prayers for a specific period; under the Sahibayn, no repetition is needed.
The commentator notes that many books prefer Abu Hanifa's view for ihtiyat (precaution).

الثياب وكان الوضوء منها؛ فإن كان الثاني وقلنا بوجوب إعادة الصلاة في تلك المدة فأولى أن نقول بوجوب الإعادة في الثياب لأنه إذا وجبت الإعادة في ثياب طاهرة فمن باب أولى أن تجب في ثياب نجسة وهو مما لا نزاع الثالث عدم لزوم الغسل مع لزوم الإعادة وفيه ما تقدم والله تعالى أعلم. قوله : فلا يتجه هذا على قوله لأنه يوجب مع الغسل الإعادة) أقول: هذا مخالف لقول المؤلف سابقاً فإنهم لا يعيدون إجماعاً» تأمل. قوله: (وفي الأول والثاني خلاف) والماء قد تحقق وهو سبب ظاهر للموت والموت فيه في نفس الأمر قد خفي، فيجب اعتباره مات فيه إحالة على السبب الظاهر عند خفاء المسبب دون الموهوم وهو الموت بسبب آخر كمن جرح إنساناً ولم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات يضاف موته إلى الجرح حتى يجب القصاص وإن احتمل موته بسبب آخر، وكذا إذا وجد قتيل في محله يضاف القتل إلى أهلها حتى تجب القسامة والدية عليهم وإن احتمل أنه قتل في موضع آخر غير أن الانتفاخ دليل التقادم فيقدر بالثلاث ولهذا يصلي على القبر إلى ثلاثة أيام على ما قيل، وعدم الانتفاخ دليل قرب العهد فقدرناه بيوم وليلة لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات لا يمكن ضبطها لتفاوتها. وأما مسألة النجاسة فقد قال المعلى بن منصور الرازي تلميذهما إنها على الخلاف، فإن كانت يابسة يعيد صلاة ثلاثة أيام، وإن كانت طرية يعيد صلاة يوم وليلة عنده فلا يحتاج إلى الفرق، ولو سلم أنها على الوفاق كما قدمنا أنه الأصح فالفرق له واضح وهو أن الثوب بمرأى عينه يقع عليه بصره فلو كانت النجاسة أصابته قبل ذلك لعلم بها بخلاف البئر فإنها غائبة عن بصره القياس
The Book of Taharah. The ruling of impurity of garments is restricted to current occurrence of impurity, not based on the past. So this does not apply according to his (Abu Hanifa's) saying because he requires repeating Salah along with washing the garment, not according to their saying (the two companions) because they do not require washing the garment at all. End. And regarding the first and second scenarios there is disagreement: According to Abu Hanifa is the detail mentioned in the book. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Ruling of impurity is at the time of knowledge of it, and they are not required to repeat any of the previous prayers nor to wash what its water had touched before knowledge. And this is qiyas because certainty is not removed by doubt, for we are certain of its purity in the past and we doubt the impurity due to the possibility that it died outside the well, or a wind threw it in, or some foolish person or children or a bird threw it in, as narrated about Abu Yusuf that he used to say with Abu Hanifa until he saw a kite with a dead mouse in its beak drop it in the well, so he retracted and accepted this opinion. And by analogy to impurity found on his garment, and to when a woman sees blood on her cotton and does not know when it happened, and to when a Muslim dies having a Christian wife and she becomes Muslim after his death claiming she converted before his death while the heirs say after, then the word is with the heirs. The common point between them is that an event is attributed to its nearest possible time. And for Abu Hanifa is istihsan: that referring to the apparent cause is obligatory when the caused matter is hidden. And this: Know that in their statement 'if they wash their garments from impurity only washing is required according to the correct view' there is investigation: because the situation either that they prayed during the mentioned period in the garments washed with that well water or they prayed in other garments and made wudhu from it. If the second, and we say repetition of prayer is obligatory in that period, then it is more fitting to say repetition is obligatory in the garments, because if repetition is obligatory for pure garments, then more so for impure garments, and there is no dispute. So if we say that the opposite of the correct view is not requiring washing the garments and the case remains, then the benefit appears but is not complete, because the assumption is they are impure, so how can it be said washing is not obligatory? And if we say the opposite of the correct view is not requiring repetition of prayer in the garments washed with its water and they prayed in them, this also no one says, for no one says one prays with impurity without excuse and does not repeat. And the difference between this garment and the well is that the garment is visible to him and others, unlike the well which is hidden from eyes, so they differ. And unlike the garments washed with well water, their ruling is the same as the well. Al-Zayla'i and those who follow him wrongly considered equality of the ruling of visible impurity on the garment and the garment washed with well water on the basis that in both there is impurity on the garment, but the difference is what we mentioned before. End. But the correct view is to drop the word 'not' from his statement 'if we say the opposite of the correct view is not requiring repetition of prayer', and thus the explanation of the rebuttal with what he mentioned does not appear, although it does not refute this point. The summary is that his statement 'upon the correct view' is either a restriction for obligation of washing or for non-repetition, both possible. And the opposite of the first is not obligating washing with non-repetition, which is the first face mentioned. And the opposite of the second is requiring repetition with requiring washing, which is apparent. And the opposite of the third is not requiring washing with requiring repetition, and there is the previous issue. And Allah knows best. His statement: 'so this does not apply according to his saying because he requires repetition with washing' I say: This contradicts the author's statement earlier 'they do not repeat by consensus'. Reflect. His statement: 'And in the first and second there is disagreement' and the water is confirmed to be an apparent cause for death, and the death itself is hidden, so it must be considered that it died there, referring to the apparent cause when the caused matter is hidden, not the imagined death by another cause. Like someone who wounds a man and he remains bedridden until death, the death is attributed to the wound so retaliation becomes obligatory even if death could be by another cause. Similarly, if a slain person is found in a locality, the killing is attributed to its people so oath and blood-money are obligatory on them even if he could have been killed elsewhere. But if there is swelling, it indicates oldness so it is estimated as three days, hence prayer is performed at the grave up to three days according to some. And lack of swelling indicates recency so we estimate it as a day and night, because less than that is hours that cannot be fixed due to variance. And as for the issue of impurity, al-Mu'alla ibn Mansur al-Razi, their student, said it is subject to disagreement: if dry, he repeats three days' prayers; if wet, he repeats one day and night according to him, so no need for distinction. And if we accept that it is agreed upon as we mentioned that it is the most correct, then the distinction is clear: the garment is in his sight, so if impurity had touched it earlier he would have known, unlike the well which is hidden from his sight. End of qiyas.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling on water from a well into which a dead mouse has fallen, specifically regarding whether past salah performed using that water must be repeated. The commentator first clarifies the matn statement: the ruling of impurity on garments is only for current impurity, not based on the past. Then he presents the two positions. According to Abu Hanifa, there is a detail: if the mouse is swollen, one must repeat prayers for three days; if not swollen, one day. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the water is ruled impure only at the time of knowledge, and no repetition of past prayers is required, nor is washing of items touched by its water before knowledge. Their proof is qiyas: certainty of past purity is not removed by doubt, and they cite multiple analogies (impurity found on a garment, a woman seeing blood, a Christian wife converting after husband's death). They also relate that Abu Yusuf retracted his earlier agreement with Abu Hanifa after seeing a kite drop a dead mouse into a well. Abu Hanifa's proof is istihsan: the apparent cause (the mouse being in the water) must be taken into account when the actual cause is hidden, similar to cases of murder where death is attributed to the wound even if other possibilities exist. The commentator then analyses the scenario where people washed their clothes with that well water and praved in them, discussing whether the clothes themselves become impure and whether prayers must be repeated. He examines various possibilities and concludes that the correct view is that if they prayed in those clothes, they must repeat the prayers because the clothes are impure and visible to them, unlike the well which is hidden. The distinction between a garment and a well is that the garment is visible, so if it were impure, the owner would have noticed, whereas the well is hidden, so we rely on the apparent cause. Later, the opinion of al-Mu'alla ibn Mansur is mentioned: he distinguishes between dry and wet impurity on a garment, but the main distinction remains between the well and a garment.

Key Terms
اليقين لا يزول بالشك (al-yaqīn lā yazūlu bi al-shakk): A legal maxim meaning that certainty is not removed by doubt; used here to argue that past purity of the water is certain, so current doubt does not affect it.
استحسان (istiḥsān): A method of juristic preference departing from qiyas for a stronger reason; here Abu Hanifa prefers the apparent cause over the hypothetical.
السبب الظاهر (al-sabab al-ẓāhir): The apparent cause that is evident (the well water being contaminated), used when the actual cause of death is hidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling that past salah must be repeated when a dead mouse is found in a well?
A) Qiyas on impurity on a garment
B) Istihsan relying on the apparent cause
C) Ijma of the companions
D) The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt'

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding well water demonstrates the tension between the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' and the principle of istihsan relying on the apparent cause.

The Authority of al-Mu'alla's Report
Analyzing the source of al-Mu'alla's report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a statement attributed to a Hanafi Imam is a direct transmission or the result of independent reasoning (qiyas).

Core Idea
Not every report from a Hanafi scholar is an authentic narration; it may be his own ijtihad.

In this passage, the commentator discusses the report of al-Mu'alla ibn Mansur regarding the well water case. Some considered it a direct transmission from Abu Hanifa, while others saw it as his own qiyas. Understanding how to weigh such reports is crucial for determining the strength of the legal position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the report
What exactly did al-Mu'alla say? The passage mentions his report on the well case.
2
Distinguish the two possibilities
The commentator presents two interpretations: (a) it is a direct narration from Abu Hanifa, (b) it is al-Mu'alla's own qiyas from the well case.
The phrase 'یحتمل كونه رواية عن الإمام' vs 'یحتمل أنه تفقه منه بطريق القياس'.
3
Review the supporting authorities
Note that al-Ispijabi and the author of al-Bada'i' considered it a narration, while al-Hakim al-Shahid and the author of al-Muhit considered it his own qiyas.
4
Determine the preferred view
The commentator sides with the view that it is his own qiyas, citing al-Hakim al-Shahid and calling it 'the truth'.
Al-Hakim al-Shahid explicitly says it is from his own understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره المعلى رحمه الله يحتمل كونه رواية عن الإمام وهو ظاهر ما ذكره الاسبيجابي وصاحب البدائع، ويحتمل أنه تفقه منه بطريق القياس على مسألة البئر وهو ظاهر ما في المحيط وهو الحق فقد قال الحاكم الشهيد: إن المعلى قال ذلك من دأب نفسه. وأما مسألة الميراث فالمرأة محتاجة إلى الاستحقاق والظاهر لا يصلح حجة لها وإنما يصلح للدفع والورثة هم الدافعون وفي المجتبى وحكم ما عجن به حكم الوضوء والغسل. وكان الصباغي يفتي بقول أبي حنيفة فيما يتعلق بالصلاة وبقولهما فيما سواه. كذا في معراج الدراية. وفي غاية البيان وما قاله أبو حنيفة احتياط في أمر العبادة، وما قالاه عمل باليقين ورفق بالناس. وفي تصحيح الشيخ قاسم رحمه الله وفي فتاوى العتابي : المختار قولهما . قلت : هو المخالف لعامة الكتب فقد رجح دليله في كثير من الكتب وقالوا: إنه الاحتياط فكان العمل عليه. وذكر الاسبيجابي أن ما عجن به قال بعضهم يلقى إلى الكلاب، وقال بعضهم يعلف المواشي، وقال بعضهم يباع من شافعي المذهب أو داودي المذهب اهـ. واختار الأول في البدائع وجزم به بصيغة قال مشايخنا يطعم للكلاب.
And what al-Mu'alla mentioned, may Allah have mercy on him, maybe a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and that is apparent from what al-Ispijabi and the author of al-Bada'i' mentioned, and it is possible that he derived it by way of qiyas from the well issue, and that is apparent from al-Muhit, and it is the truth. For al-Hakim al-Shahid said: Al-Mu'alla said that from his own understanding. As for the inheritance issue, the woman needs entitlement and the apparent does not suffice as proof for her; it only suffices for defense, and the heirs are the defenders. And in al-Mujtaba: The ruling of what is kneaded with it is like the ruling of wudhu and ghusl. And al-Sabbaghi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa's saying regarding matters related to prayer, and according to their saying regarding other matters. So in Miraj al-Dirayah. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: What Abu Hanifa said is ihtiyat in matters of worship, and what they said is acting on certainty and kindness to people. And in the correction of Shaykh Qasim, may Allah have mercy on him, and in Fatawa al-Attabi: The chosen is their saying (the two companions). I say: That contradicts the majority of books, for they ranked his proof higher in many books and said: It is ihtiyat, so action is upon it. And al-Ispijabi mentioned that what is kneaded with it: some said feed it to dogs, some said feed it to livestock, some said sell it to a Shafi'i or Dawoodi. End. And he chose the first in al-Bada'i' and confirmed it with the phrase: Our shaykhs said feed it to dogs.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the well water issue. It begins by examining the report of al-Mu'alla ibn Mansur (a student of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the well case. The commentator notes that al-Mu'alla's statement might be a direct narration from Abu Hanifa, or it might be his own qiyas from the well case. Al-Hakim al-Shahid says al-Mu'alla derived it from his own understanding. Then a short inheritance case is mentioned: a Christian wife who converts after her husband's death claiming she converted before his death; the apparent evidence is not sufficient to prove her entitlement; the heirs are the ones defending. Then it moves to the ruling on dough (عجين) that is made with water from such a well. Al-Mujtaba says the ruling of the dough is like the ruling of wudhu and ghusl i.e., it becomes impure. Al-Sabbaghi used to differentiate: for prayer-related matters he followed Abu Hanifa (requiring ihtiyat), for other matters he followed the two companions (more lenient). Some sources say the chosen view is that of the two companions, but the commentator disagrees, stating that the majority of books prefer Abu Hanifa's proof as ihtiyat and act upon it. Finally, various opinions are given for disposing of the dough: feed to dogs, feed livestock, or sell to a Shafi'i or Dawoodi (who might consider it pure). The author of al-Bada'i' chose the first (feed to dogs).

Key Terms
احتياط (iḥtiyāṭ): Precaution; Abu Hanifa's approach is precautionary in matters of worship, requiring repetition of Salah.
عجين (ʿajīn): Dough (usually flour mixed with water). Here it refers to dough made with water from the impure well.
المختار قولهما (al-mukhtār qawluhumā): The chosen (preferred) opinion is that of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
رواية (riwāyah): Narration, direct transmission from an Imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred view of the commentator regarding al-Mu'alla's report?
A) It is a direct narration from Abu Hanifa.
B) It is his own qiyas.
C) It is a weak report.
D) It is a hadith.

Key Takeaway
Reports from Hanafi students must be critically assessed; they may be their own ijtihad rather than direct narrations.

The Ruling on Dough Made with Impure Water and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling on dough and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dough made with water from a well containing a dead mouse and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's view and that of his two companions.

Core Idea
Dough made with impure water is impure, but there is disagreement on whether precaution or leniency applies.

Having assessed the authority of al-Mu'alla's report, the passage now turns to the practical ruling on dough (عجين) made with water from an impure well. The key dispute is between Abu Hanifa's precautionary approach (ihtiyat) in prayer matters and the more lenient approach of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad based on certainty (yaqin). Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
The dough is considered impure, like wudhu and ghusl with that water.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa requires ihtiyat (e.g., repeat prayer if prayed with that water), while the two companions follow certainty (no need to repeat).
3
Examine the practice of al-Sabbaghi
He combined both: follow Abu Hanifa for prayer matters, follow the two companions for other matters.
4
Determine the preferred view
The commentator states that despite some sources choosing the two companions' view, the majority prefer Abu Hanifa's proof and act on it for ihtiyat.
5
Note the disposal options
Feed to dogs, livestock, or sell to non-Hanafis. Al-Bada'i' chooses feeding dogs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يقين (yaqīn): Certainty; the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt, used by the two companions.

Key Takeaway
